Greeting and Condolences to Non-Muslims
Rulings on greeting, dua, and condolences for non-Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on returning salam, initiating salam, making dua, and offering condolences to non-Muslims.

Core Idea
Return salam minimally, do not initiate, do not pray for forgiveness but for guidance; condolences focus on the living family.

The source text provides detailed etiquette for interacting with non-Muslims in greeting and condolences. These rulings balance maintaining Islamic identity with civic respect. This lesson unpacks the specific wording and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling for returning salam
When a dhimmi gives salam, the ruling is to return with 'wa alaykum' and not to add more. Proof: the Prophet did not add more.
The text says 'ولا يزده على قوله وعليك'
2
Identify the ruling for initiating salam
It is not permissible to initiate salam to a dhimmi because it honors him. Exception: if there is a need (e.g., to greet before a request), then it is permissible.
The text says 'لا يبدؤه بالسلام لأن فيه تعظيما له'
3
Identify the ruling for dua for a non-Muslim
One should not pray for forgiveness (maghfirah) but may pray for guidance (huda). Praying for long life is disputed: some allow because it benefits Muslims via jizya, some disallow.
The text says 'لا يدعو له بالمغفرة ويدعو له بالهدى'
4
Identify the ruling for condolences (ta'ziyah) for a non-Muslim
When a kafir dies, one says to his family: 'May Allah replace him with something better and grant you a Muslim child.' Reason: jizya purifies the deceased in some sense.
The text gives specific wording for Muslim condolence as well.
5
Note the differences between mourning for a Muslim vs. non-Muslim
For a Muslim, the condolence includes 'may Allah increase your reward' etc. For a non-Muslim, the focus is on the living, not the dead.
This highlights that we do not ask Allah to forgive the deceased non-Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وعيادته) يعني تجوز عيادة الذمي المريض لما روي أن يهوديـاً مـرض بـجـوار النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : قوموا بنا نعود جارنا اليهودي فقاموا ودخل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وقعد عند رأسه وقال له : قل أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وأن محمداً رسول الله فنظر المريض إلى أبيه فقال : أجبه . فنطق بالشهادة فقال : الحمد الله الذي أنقذ نسمة من النار الحديث. ولأن العيادة نوع من البر وهي من محاسن الإسلام فلا بأس بها. ويرد السلام على الذمي ولا يزده على قوله «وعليك» لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام لم يزده على ذلك. ولا يبدؤه بالسلام لأن فيه تعظيماً له فإن كان له إليه حاجة فلا بأس ببداءته ولا يدعو له بالمغفرة ويدعو له بالهدى، ولو دعا له بطول العمر قيل يجوز لأن فيه نفعاً للمسلمين بالجزية، وقيل لا يجوز، وعلى هذا الدعاء بالعافية. وهذا إذا كان من أهل الكتاب ولو كان مجوسياً لا يعوده لأنه أبعد عن الإسلام، وقيل يعوده لأن فيه إظهار محاسن الإسلام وترغيبه فيه . واختلفوا في عيادة الفاسق والأصح أنه لا بأس به لأنه مسلم والعيادة في حق المسلمين. وإذا مات الكافر قيل لوالده أو لقريبه في تعزيته أخلف الله عليك خيراً منه وأصلحك ورزقك ولداً مسلماً لأن الجزية تطهر. ويقول في تعزية المسلم أعظم الله أجرك وأحسن عزاءك ورحم ميثك وأكثر عددك. وفي النوازل: ولا بأس بأن يصل الرجل المسلم المشرك قريباً كان أو بعيداً، محارباً كان أو ذمياً. وأراد بالمحارب المستأمن فأما إذا كان غير مستأمن فلا ينبغي له أن يصله بشيء. وفي الذخيرة: إذا كان حربياً في دار الحرب وكان الحال حال صلح فلا بأس بأن يصله. واختلفوا هل يكره لنا أن نقبل هدية المشرك أو لا تقبل ؟ ذكر فيه قولان وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند : مسلم دعاه نصراني إلى داره ضيفاً حل له أن يذهب معه . . وفي النوازل : المجوسي أو النصراني إذا دعا رجلاً إلى طعام تكره الإجابة وإن قال اشتريت اللحم من السوق فإن كان الداعي يهودياً فلا بأس .
He said: And visiting him, meaning it is permissible to visit a sick dhimmi because it is narrated that a Jew fell ill near the Prophet, so he said: 'Stand, let us go visit our Jewish neighbor.' They stood, the Prophet entered and sat at his head and said to him: 'Say there is no god but Allah and Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah.' The patient looked at his father, who said: 'Answer him.' So he uttered the shahada, and the Prophet said: 'Praise be to Allah who saved a soul from the fire.' And because visiting is a kind of piety and is among the good qualities of Islam, so there is no harm. And one returns salam to a dhimmi without adding more than 'wa alaykum' because the Prophet did not add more. And one does not initiate salam because that is honoring him. If one needs something from him, then initiating is permissible. And one does not pray for his forgiveness but prays for his guidance. If one prays for his long life, some say it is permissible because it benefits Muslims through jizya, and some say it is not permissible, and similarly for praying for well-being. This is if he is of Ahl al-Kitab; if he is a Zoroastrian, he is not visited because he is farther from Islam, though some say he is visited because it shows the good qualities of Islam and attracts him. There is a difference of opinion on visiting a sinner (fasiq); the correct view is that it is permissible because he is a Muslim, and visiting is for Muslims. If a kafir dies, one says to his father or relative: 'May Allah replace him with something better for you, and reform you, and grant you a Muslim child' because jizya purifies. And in offering condolences to a Muslim, one says: 'May Allah increase your reward, and perfect your consolation, and have mercy on your deceased, and increase your number.' And in al-Nawazil: There is no harm if a Muslim gives a gift to a polytheist, whether relative or not, whether warner or dhimmi. He meant by 'warner' musta'min. As for a non-musta'min, he should not give him anything. In al-Dhakhira: If he is a belligerent in dar al-harb and there is peace, there is no harm in giving. They differed: is it disliked to accept a gift from a polytheist or not? Two opinions. In Fatawa of Ahl Samarkand: If a Muslim is invited by a Christian to his house, it is permissible for him to go. In al-Nawazil: If a Zoroastrian or Christian invites a man to food, the answer is disliked, unless he says 'I bought the meat from the market'; if the host is a Jew, there is no harm.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This is a comprehensive ruling with proof on social interactions with non-Muslims. The permissibility of visiting a sick dhimmi is proven by the Prophet's own action. The text then details proper conduct: returning salam (only 'wa alaykum'), not initiating salam unless necessary, not praying for forgiveness but for guidance, and the disputed issues of praying for long life. Visiting a Zoroastrian is disputed; the preferred view is it is permissible as da'wah. Visiting a sinful Muslim is permissible. Condolences: for a kafir, pray for a better replacement for the family; for a Muslim, specific du'a. Giving gifts to non-Muslims is permissible except to non-musta'min in dar al-harb. Accepting gifts has two opinions. Attending invitations: general permissibility for Jews, but for Zoroastrians and Christians, it is disliked unless the food source is known to be permissible.

Key Terms
تعزية (ta'ziyah): Offering condolences; specific wording for Muslim vs. non-Muslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should you say when offering condolences to the family of a deceased dhimmi?
A) May Allah forgive him and grant him paradise.
B) May Allah give you patience and reward you.
C) May Allah replace him with something better for you and grant you a Muslim child.
D) We belong to Allah and to Him we return.

Key Takeaway
Greet non-Muslims minimally, do not initiate, and offer condolences that focus on the living, not the deceased's forgiveness.

Gifts and Invitations to/from Non-Muslims
Rulings on gifts and invitations involving non-Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on giving gifts to non-Muslims, accepting gifts from them, and attending their invitations, including the disputes.

Core Idea
Gifts to non-Muslims are permissible, but accepting gifts is disputed; invitations depend on the host's faith and food source.

The final part of the passage addresses financial and social transactions with non-Muslims: giving gifts, accepting gifts, and attending dinner invitations. These rulings involve nuanced considerations of war and peace, food permissibility, and potential fitna. This lesson breaks down each scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on giving gifts to non-Muslims
It is permissible (la ba'sa) to give a gift to a polytheist, whether relative or not, and whether a belligerent (musta'min) or dhimmi. Exception: a non-musta'min in dar al-harb cannot receive gifts.
The text cites al-Nawazil and al-Dhakhira.
2
Identify the ruling on accepting gifts from non-Muslims
There is disagreement: some hold it is makruh, others permissible. The text reports two opinions without giving preference.
The text says 'اختلفوا هل يكره أن نقبل هدية المشرك أو لا تقبل؟'
3
Identify the ruling on attending invitations from non-Muslims
If a Muslim is invited by a Christian (Nasrani), it is permissible (halla) to go. If invited by a Zoroastrian or Christian, it is disliked (tukrah), unless the food is bought from the market (known to be halal). If invited by a Jew, there is no harm.
The text gives a distinction based on the host's religiosity regarding food.
4
Understand the reasoning behind the distinctions
Jews are considered more reliable in meat slaughter? Or the ruling is based on the assumption that Jews may slaughter properly? The text does not explain, but it is a nuance.
The ruling likely relates to the permissibility of eating their slaughtered meat (for Ahl al-Kitab, it is halal; for Zoroastrians, it is not).
5
Note the ikhtilaf on accepting gifts and apply tarjih thinking
The mufta bihi position in the later Hanafi school is that accepting a gift from a non-Muslim is permissible unless it is from a harbi or leads to fitna. This is based on the Prophet accepting gifts from non-Muslims.
The text does not give tarjih, but general principles apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وعيادته) يعني تجوز عيادة الذمي المريض لما روي أن يهوديـاً مـرض بـجـوار النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : قوموا بنا نعود جارنا اليهودي فقاموا ودخل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وقعد عند رأسه وقال له : قل أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وأن محمداً رسول الله فنظر المريض إلى أبيه فقال : أجبه . فنطق بالشهادة فقال : الحمد الله الذي أنقذ نسمة من النار الحديث. ولأن العيادة نوع من البر وهي من محاسن الإسلام فلا بأس بها. ويرد السلام على الذمي ولا يزده على قوله «وعليك» لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام لم يزده على ذلك. ولا يبدؤه بالسلام لأن فيه تعظيماً له فإن كان له إليه حاجة فلا بأس ببداءته ولا يدعو له بالمغفرة ويدعو له بالهدى، ولو دعا له بطول العمر قيل يجوز لأن فيه نفعاً للمسلمين بالجزية، وقيل لا يجوز، وعلى هذا الدعاء بالعافية. وهذا إذا كان من أهل الكتاب ولو كان مجوسياً لا يعوده لأنه أبعد عن الإسلام، وقيل يعوده لأن فيه إظهار محاسن الإسلام وترغيبه فيه . واختلفوا في عيادة الفاسق والأصح أنه لا بأس به لأنه مسلم والعيادة في حق المسلمين. وإذا مات الكافر قيل لوالده أو لقريبه في تعزيته أخلف الله عليك خيراً منه وأصلحك ورزقك ولداً مسلماً لأن الجزية تطهر. ويقول في تعزية المسلم أعظم الله أجرك وأحسن عزاءك ورحم ميثك وأكثر عددك. وفي النوازل: ولا بأس بأن يصل الرجل المسلم المشرك قريباً كان أو بعيداً، محارباً كان أو ذمياً. وأراد بالمحارب المستأمن فأما إذا كان غير مستأمن فلا ينبغي له أن يصله بشيء. وفي الذخيرة: إذا كان حربياً في دار الحرب وكان الحال حال صلح فلا بأس بأن يصله. واختلفوا هل يكره لنا أن نقبل هدية المشرك أو لا تقبل ؟ ذكر فيه قولان وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند : مسلم دعاه نصراني إلى داره ضيفاً حل له أن يذهب معه . . وفي النوازل : المجوسي أو النصراني إذا دعا رجلاً إلى طعام تكره الإجابة وإن قال اشتريت اللحم من السوق فإن كان الداعي يهودياً فلا بأس .

Commentary
This is a comprehensive ruling with proof on social interactions with non-Muslims. The permissibility of visiting a sick dhimmi is proven by the Prophet's own action. Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Muslim lands with safe conduct (aman).
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim from a land at war with Muslims, without aman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the source, when is it NOT permissible to give a gift to a non-Muslim?
A) When he is a dhimmi.
B) When he is a musta'min.
C) When he is a non-musta'min in dar al-harb.
D) When he is a relative.

Key Takeaway
Gifts to non-Muslims are permissible except for enemy combatants; accepting gifts is allowed; invitations require checking the host and food source.

The Permissibility of Castrating Animals in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on the permissibility of castrating animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on castrating animals and identify the prophetic evidence that supports it.

Core Idea
Castrating animals is permissible (mubah) in the Hanafi school because the Prophet sacrificed castrated rams.

This lesson examines a concise ruling from the Hanafi tradition regarding a practical matter: the castration of animals. The passage presents a clear proof directly from the practice of the Prophet himself. Understanding this ruling not only clarifies the school's position but also illustrates how prophetic action (fi'l) serves as a source of law in the Hanafi methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is 'khasy al-baha'im' — the castration of animals. The Hanafi ruling given is 'ja'iz' (permissible).
The text opens with 'wa khasy al-baha'im' as a continuation of a matn.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The evidence cited is the Prophet's action: 'he sacrificed two rams' (that were castrated). This is a direct reference to a hadith where the Prophet offered castrated animals as sacrifices.
The phrase 'li-annahu' indicates that the following action is the reason for permissibility.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how it proves the ruling)
The Prophet's action (fi'l) is considered a source of legislation. By sacrificing castrated animals, he implicitly approved the act of castration itself, as sacrificial animals must be sound.
In Hanafi usul, the Prophet's action can indicate permissibility (ibahah) unless there is evidence to the contrary.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (difference of opinion)
Some other schools may prohibit castration based on the general prohibition of altering Allah's creation. The Hanafi school addresses this by giving precedence to the specific prophetic practice.
This is a legitimate scholarly difference (ikhtilaf).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preferred position)
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is permissibility, as established in the zahir al-riwayah.
The text does not mention any conditions; the ruling is general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وخصي البهائم) يعني يجوز لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام ضحى بكبشين
He said: And castrating animals, meaning it is permissible because the Prophet sacrificed two rams (and they were castrated).
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This is a short ruling with proof regarding the castration of animals. The Hanafi position is that it is permissible (ja'iz) based on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him), who sacrificed two rams that were castrated. This indicates that castration does not render an animal unsuitable for sacrifice or consumption. The ruling is general, implying that castration is allowed for both sacrificial and non-sacrificial animals, though some scholars may restrict it to necessity. The text does not specify conditions or details.

Key Terms
خصي البهائم (khasy al-baha'im): Castration of animals; permissible in Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is castration of animals permissible?
A) Because it benefits the animal.
B) Because the Prophet sacrificed castrated rams.
C) Because it is necessary for breeding.
D) Because there is a Qur'anic command.

Key Takeaway
Castrating animals is permissible in the Hanafi school, established by the Prophet's own practice of sacrificing castrated rams.

Social Interactions with Non-Muslims: Consolation, Gifts, and Invitations
Rulings on social interactions with non-Muslims: ta'ziya, gifts, invitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on consoling a non-Muslim, giving gifts to a non-Muslim, and accepting gifts and invitations from non-Muslims, and identify the relevant conditions and differences of opinion.

Core Idea
Interacting with non-Muslims is permitted with proper conditions; gifts may be given and accepted for da'wah benefit.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses practical questions about social interactions with non-Muslims: how to console them at a death, whether to give them gifts, and whether to accept their gifts and invitations. These rulings reflect the Hanafi school's balanced approach—allowing social bonds while maintaining Islamic identity and avoiding religious compromise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
The text covers three distinct situations: (1) wording for consoling a non-Muslim, (2) giving gifts (sila) to non-Muslims, and (3) accepting gifts and invitations from non-Muslims. Identify each in the source.
2
Analyze conditions for giving gifts
Note that giving gifts is permitted only when the recipient is a musta'min (protected by safe conduct) or when a state of peace exists. If the non-Muslim is a harbi not under aman, giving is not befitting.
The text makes a distinction: harbi in dar al-harb with peace is like musta'min.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf on accepting gifts
The text explicitly states there are two opinions among Hanafis: some dislike accepting gifts from polytheists, others permit it. Identify the practical implication of each view.
The Samarkand fatwa on invitations leans toward permissibility.
4
Examine the invitation rulings
The text differentiates based on the inviter's religion and the source of food. A Christian's invitation is permissible; a Magian or Christian's food invitation is disliked unless the inviter is a Jew and claims market meat.
The condition 'if he says I bought the meat from the market' alleviates the dislike.
5
Summarize the operative rulings
Based on the later fatwa collections (Nawazil, Dhakhira), the mufta bihi position is permissibility of giving gifts to musta'min and in peace, and permissibility of accepting gifts and invitations with conditions.
The text does not give explicit tarjih, but the Samarkand fatwa supports permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا مات الكافر قيل لوالده أو لقريبه في تعزيته أخلف الله عليك خيراً منه وأصلحك ورزقك ولداً مسلماً لأن الجزية تطهر. ويقول في تعزية المسلم أعظم الله أجرك وأحسن عزاءك ورحم ميثك وأكثر عددك. وفي النوازل: ولا بأس بأن يصل الرجل المسلم المشرك قريباً كان أو بعيداً، محارباً كان أو ذمياً. وأراد بالمحارب المستأمن فأما إذا كان غير مستأمن فلا ينبغي له أن يصله بشيء. وفي الذخيرة: إذا كان حربياً في دار الحرب وكان الحال حال صلح فلا بأس بأن يصله. واختلفوا هل يكره لنا أن نقبل هدية المشرك أو لا نقبل ؟ ذكر فيه قولان وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند : مسلم دعاه نصراني إلى داره ضيفاً حل له أن يذهب معه . . وفي النوازل : المجوسي أو النصراني إذا دعا رجلاً إلى طعام تكره الإجابة وإن قال اشتريت اللحم من السوق فإن كان الداعي يهودياً فلا بأس .
And when a non-believer (kafir) dies, one says to his parent or relative in consolation: 'May Allah replace you with something better than him, and improve your state, and grant you a Muslim child,' because jizya purifies. And one says in consoling a Muslim: 'May Allah increase your reward, and make your condolence good, and have mercy on your deceased, and increase your number.' And in al-Nawazil: There is no harm if a Muslim man gives a gift (sila) to a polytheist, whether relative or distant, whether harbi (warring) or dhimmi. He meant by harbi the musta'min (one given safe conduct). As for one who is not a musta'min, it is not befitting for him to give him anything. And in al-Dhakhira: If there is a harbi in dar al-harb and the situation is one of peace, then there is no harm in giving him something. And they differed: is it disliked for us to accept the gift of a polytheist or not? Two opinions are mentioned. And in the Fatawa of the people of Samarkand: If a Muslim is invited by a Christian to his house as a guest, it is permissible for him to go with him. And in al-Nawazil: If a Magian or Christian invites a man to food, answering is disliked. If he says, 'I bought the meat from the market,' then if the inviter is a Jew, there is no harm.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage covers multiple rulings on social interactions with non-Muslims. When consoling a non-Muslim for the death of a relative, one should express hope for a better replacement and a Muslim child, as jizya purifies the non-Muslim's status. For Muslims, the traditional words of condolence are used. Giving gifts (sila) to polytheists (mushrikin) is permissible if they are musta'min (protected by safe conduct) or if in a state of peace; otherwise, it is not recommended. Accepting gifts from polytheists has two opinions: some dislike it, others permit. Accepting invitations: if a Christian invites a Muslim, going is permissible (according to Samarkand fatwa). If a Magian or Christian invites for food, it is disliked unless the inviter is a Jew and claims the meat is from the market, then no harm. These rulings balance kindness with caution to avoid religious compromise.

Key Terms
تعزية (ta'ziya): Consolation offered to the bereaved family of a deceased person.
المستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim given safe conduct (aman) to enter Muslim lands temporarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on going to a Christian's house when invited as a guest?
A) It is completely prohibited.
B) It is disliked.
C) It is permissible.
D) It is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh permits social interaction with non-Muslims—including consolation, giving gifts, and accepting gifts and invitations—with specific conditions and recognition of legitimate ikhtilaf.

Castration of Animals: Permissibility and Proof
Ruling on castration of animals with dalā'il and methodological response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on castration of animals, identify the dalil from the Prophet's sacrifice, and explain the response to the objection that the proof only shows permissibility of sacrifice, not castration.

Core Idea
Castrating animals for benefit is permissible, proven by the Prophet's own sacrifice of castrated rams.

The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that castration of animals is permissible. He supports this with the hadith that the Prophet sacrificed castrated rams, and he argues that since the meat becomes better and breeding is controlled, it is good. A hypothetical objection arises: the hadith only proves permissibility of sacrificing a castrated animal, not the act of castration itself. The answer is given by analogy to branding animals for the owner's benefit, which was practiced in the Prophet's time. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the evidence, and the method of derivation.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the subject (castration of animals) and the hukm from the text (permissible/jā'iz).
The author uses 'يجوز' (it is permitted).
2
Locate the primary dalīl
Find the hadith reference: the Prophet sacrificed two castrated rams (mawju'ayn).
The word 'الموجوء' means castrated.
3
Analyse the objection and response
Understand the objection: permissibility of sacrifice does not entail permissibility of castration. Then follow the Hanafi response: qiyas (analogy) to branding for benefit, which was common.
This shows how usul is applied to derive a ruling from a related action.
4
Extract the general principle
From the response, the principle is that causing pain to an animal for a legitimate benefit to the owner or animal is permissible.
This principle is elaborated in al-Muhit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وخصي البهائم) يعني يجوز لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام ضحى بكبشين أملحين موجوأين والموجوء هو الخصي ولأن لحمه يطيب به ويترك النكاح فكان حسناً. ولك أن تقول الدليل لا يفيد جواز الفعل وإنما يفيد جواز التضحية به ولا يلزم من جواز التضحية جواز الفعل، والجواب أن البهائم كانت تكثر في زمنه فتكوى بالنار لأجل المنفعة للمالك فكذا يجوز هذا الفعل لتعود المنفعة للمالك. وفي الصحاح : جمع خصي هو خصا بكسر الخاء والرجل خصي وخصية اهـ. قال العيني : والخصيان بضم الخاء جمع خصي. وفي المحيط : أن الأصل إيصال الألم إلى الحيوان لمصلحة تعود إلى الحيوان يجوز ولا بأس بكي إليها ثم للعلامة، ويكره كسب الخصي من بني آدم وقتل النملة قيل لا بأس به مطلقاً. وقيل إن بدأت بالأذى فلا بأس به، وإن لم تبتدئ يكره وهو المختار، ويكره إلقاؤها في الماء. وقتل القملة يجوز بكل حال . قرية فيها كلاب كثيرة ولأهل القرية منها ضرر يؤمر أرباب الكلاب بأن يقتلوا كلابهم لأن دفع الضرر واجب وإن أبوا ألزمهم القاضي ولا ينبغي أن يتخذ في بيته كلباً إلا كلب الحراسة الهرة إذا كانت مؤذية يذبحها بالسكين ويكره ضربها وفرك إذنها اهـ. وأطلق المؤلف في البهائم فشمل الخيل. وفي الخانية ويكره خصي الفرس. وذكر شمس الأئمة في شرحه أن خصي الفرس حرام اهـ وفي الخانية : لا بأس بثقب أذن الطفل اهـ.
He (the author) said: (And castration of animals) meaning it is permissible because the Prophet sacrificed two rams that were horned and castrated (mawju’ayn), and mawju’ is the castrated one. And because its meat thereby becomes good, and it avoids breeding, so it was good. But you might say: The proof does not indicate the permissibility of the act, but rather the permissibility of sacrificing with it, and permissibility of sacrifice does not necessarily imply permissibility of the act. The answer is that animals were abundant in his time, so they were branded with fire for the benefit of the owner; similarly, this act is permitted for the benefit of the owner. And in al-Sihah: The plural of khasi is khisa with kasra on the kha, and a man is khasi and khisya. Al-Ayni said: al-Khusyan with damma on the kha is the plural of khasi. And in al-Muhit: The principle is that causing pain to an animal for a benefit that returns to the animal is permissible, and branding is permissible. Then for marking, and the earning of a castrator of humans is disliked. Killing an ant: some say it is absolutely permissible. Some say if it starts harm, then no harm; if it does not start, it is disliked, and this is the chosen view. And throwing it into water is disliked. Killing a louse is permissible in any case. In a village with many dogs causing harm, the owners are ordered to kill them because removing harm is obligatory; if they refuse, the judge compels them. One should not keep a dog in his house except a guard dog. A cat, if harmful, is slaughtered with a knife, and it is disliked to beat it or twist its ears. The author generalized animals, including horses. In al-Khaniyya, castration of a horse is disliked. Shams al-A'ima mentioned in his commentary that castration of a horse is haram. In al-Khaniyya, there is no harm in piercing a child's ear.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage discusses castration of animals, beginning with general permissibility based on the Prophet's sacrifice of castrated rams. A potential objection that sacrificing a castrated animal does not prove the act of castration is permissible is answered by analogy to branding for benefit. The principle is that causing pain for a benefit that returns to the animal (or owner) is permissible. However, for horses there is ikhtilaf: some Hanafi texts say disliked (makruh), Shams al-A'ima says haram. Castration of humans (as an occupation) is disliked. Other rulings on harming animals: killing ants is permissible if they start harm, otherwise disliked; killing lice is always permissible; harmful dogs must be killed if they cause harm; cats may be slaughtered if harmful, but beating or twisting ears is disliked. Piercing a child's ear is permissible. These rulings balance animal welfare with human need.

Key Terms
الخصي (al-khasi): Castrated animal, or the act of castration.
الموجوء (al-mawju'): A castrated ram, specifically one whose testicles are crushed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence cited for the permissibility of castrating animals?
A) Quranic verse on animal welfare
B) The Prophet sacrificing castrated rams
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Qiyas on slaughtering animals

Key Takeaway
Castration of animals for benefit is permissible, supported by the Prophet's practice and analogical reasoning.

Rulings on Harmful Creatures and Piercing Ears
Subsidiary rulings on harmful animals and ear piercing, derived from the principle of benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on killing ants, lice, harmful dogs and cats, and piercing a child's ear, and identify the underlying principle of balancing harm and benefit.

Core Idea
Islamic law permits causing limited harm to animals when necessary, but cruelty is disliked; piercing ears for adornment is permissible.

The passage continues with a series of practical rulings derived from the principle that causing pain for benefit is allowed. It covers ants, lice, dogs, cats, and ends with the permissibility of piercing a child's ear. Each ruling balances the creature's harm or benefit against the prohibition of unnecessary cruelty. This lesson presents these specific applications and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
From al-Muhit: causing pain to an animal for a benefit that returns to the animal is permissible.
This is the overarching rule.
2
Rulings on ants
Two opinions: absolute permissibility, or disliked if not initiated harm; the chosen view is that it is permissible if they start harm, otherwise disliked.
Throwing them in water is also disliked.
3
Ruling on lice
Lice can be killed in any case because they are harmful.
There is no restriction.
4
Rulings on dogs and cats
Harmful dogs in a village: owners must kill them to remove harm; judge can compel. A guard dog is an exception. A harmful cat may be slaughtered with a knife, but beating and twisting ears is disliked.
Note the distinction: cats are slaughtered, not beaten.
5
Ruling on piercing ears
Piercing a child's ear is permissible (no harm).
This is from al-Khaniyya.

The Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Castration of Horses
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on a specific case: horse castration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions within the Hanafi school regarding castration of horses, and explain why this ikhtilaf exists.

Core Idea
While general castration is permissible, horses have special status: some Hanafi scholars say makruh, others say haram.

After stating the general permissibility of castration, the author notes an exception: the horse. He cites two views from Hanafi sources: al-Khaniyya records it as makruh (disliked), while Shams al-A'ima declares it haram (prohibited). This lesson examines the basis of this internal disagreement and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the general ruling
Recall from Lesson 1 that castration of animals is generally permissible.
The author's generalisation includes all animals, but then he qualifies.
2
Identify the exception
The author says: 'وأطلق المؤلف في البهائم فشمل الخيل' (the author generalized and included horses).
This signals that the general ruling may not fully apply.
3
State the two positions
From al-Khaniyya: it is makruh. From Shams al-A'ima: it is haram.
The text does not explicitly choose one; the mufta bihi is often the intermediate view (makruh).
4
Analyse possible reasons for the ikhtilaf
Horses have special value in Islamic culture (jihad, transportation). The extra status may elevate the prohibition.
Some may argue that the benefit from castration does not outweigh the honor of the horse.

Source Text

Ruling on Piercing a Child's Ear in the Hanafi School
States the permissibility of piercing a child's ear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on piercing a child's ear and explain the basis of its permissibility.

Core Idea
Piercing a child's ear for ornaments is mubah (permissible) in the Hanafi school, based on custom and tolerated minor harm.

This concise ruling from al-Khaniyya addresses a common practice: piercing a child's ear for earrings. Although it involves some discomfort, the Hanafi school considers it permissible due to widespread custom ('urf) and the minimal nature of the harm. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its legal weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is piercing a child's ear. The ruling is 'la ba'sa bihi' (no harm), i.e., mubah (permissible).
2
Note the source authority
The ruling is from al-Khaniyya, a Hanafi fatwa compilation, thus from the fatawa mutakhkhirin category.
This is not from the core Zahir al-Riwayah texts.
3
Understand the basis
The basis is 'urf (custom) and tasamuh (tolerance) of slight pain for adornment. The harm is minimal and the practice is widespread.
Hanafi usul allows custom to inform rulings when no explicit text exists.
4
Recognize the authority weight
This is from later fatwa works, not from the core zuhur riwayah, but it is relied upon as the mufta bihi position.
The ruling is mukhtalaf fih (disputed) across schools, but within Hanafi it is settled.
5
Consider cross-madhhab differences
Some schools may consider it makruh due to causing unnecessary pain, but the Hanafi view gives weight to custom and the benefit of adornment.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is that Hanafi parents may do it without religious discouragement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : لا بأس بثقب أذن الطفل اهـ
In al-Khaniyya: There is no harm in piercing a child's ear, end.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This is a concise ruling statement from al-Khaniyya, a Hanafi fatwa work. It declares that piercing a child's ear for the purpose of wearing earrings or ornaments is permissible (la ba'sa = mubah). This is based on 'urf (custom) as it is a common practice and involves a minor harm that is tolerated for adornment.

Key Terms
ثقب أذن الطفل (thaqb udhun al-tifl): Piercing the ear of a child, typically to wear earrings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Piercing a child's ear for ornaments is permissible in the Hanafi school due to custom and minimal harm.

Castrating Animals: Permissibility and the Horse Dispute
Determines permissibility of castrating animals and the explicit ruling on horses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on castrating animals and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute concerning horses.

Core Idea
Castrating animals for benefit is permissible; horse castration is disputed between makruh and haram.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a practical issue: is it permissible to castrate animals? The author presents a general ruling based on benefit, but then highlights a key intra-Hanafi disagreement over horses. You will learn the proofs and reasoning behind these positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the general ruling
Determine what act is being discussed (castration of animals) and the default ruling (permissible for benefit).
The text begins by defining terms and stating that castrated meat is good.
2
Trace the proof: objection and response
Understand the objection that permissibility to sacrifice a castrated animal does not prove permissibility to castrate. Then see the response using analogy to branding for benefit.
This is an example of istihsan based on 'urf and necessity.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute on horses
Identify that the author's general mention of 'bahaim' includes horses, and then note the two views: al-Khaniyya says makruh, Shams al-Aimmah says haram.
The text does not resolve the dispute, so both positions are valid for study.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical consequence: if haram, castrating a horse is sinful; if makruh, it is disliked but not sinful.
This affects the fatwa given to a query about horse castration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أملحين [؟] موجوأين [؟] والموجوء هو الخصي ولأن لحمه يطيب به ويترك النكاح فكان حسناً. ولك أن تقول الدليل لا يفيد جواز الفعل وإنما يفيد جواز التضحية به ولا يلزم من جواز التضحية جواز الفعل، والجواب أن البهائم كانت تكثر في زمنه فتكوى بالنار لأجل المنفعة للمالك فكذا يجوز هذا الفعل لتعود المنفعة للمالك. وفي الصحاح : جمع خصي هو خصا بكسر الخاء والرجل خصي وخصية اهـ. قال العيني : والخصيان بضم الخاء جمع خصي. وفي المحيط : أن الأصل إيصال الألم إلى الحيوان لمصلحة تعود إلى الحيوان يجوز ولا بأس بكي إليها ثم للعلامة، ويكره كسب الخصي من بني آدم. وأطلق المؤلف في البهائم فشمل الخيل. وفي الخانية ويكره خصي الفرس. وذكر شمس الأئمة في شرحه أن خصي الفرس حرام اهـ
Two types of castration? The castrated one is the khasi, because its meat becomes good and it abandons mating, so it is good. You may say: The evidence does not indicate the permissibility of the act, but rather indicates the permissibility of sacrificing it, and permissibility of sacrificing does not necessitate permissibility of the act. The answer: Animals were abundant in his time, so they were branded with fire for the benefit of the owner; similarly, this act is permitted for the benefit of the owner. In al-Sihah: The plural of khasi is khisa with kasr of kha, and the man is khasi and khisyah, end. Al-Aini said: Al-khisyān with damm of kha is the plural of khasi. In al-Muhit: The principle is that causing pain to an animal for a benefit that returns to the animal is permissible, and there is no harm in branding it, then for the mark. And the earnings of a castrator from humans are disliked. And the author said in al-Bahaim that it includes horses. In al-Khaniyya: Castrating a horse is disliked. Shams al-Aimmah mentioned in his commentary that castrating a horse is forbidden, end.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on castrating animals. The author begins with a linguistic note: 'almulhayn al-mawjuwayn'—though the OCR is unclear, it likely refers to two types of castrated animals. The castrated animal (mawju' = khasi) is considered good because its meat becomes tastier and it stops mating, which is beneficial. Then the commentator presents an objection: the evidence (likely from hadith about sacrificing a castrated animal) only proves permissibility of sacrifice, not of the castration act itself. The response is an analogy: just as branding animals with fire for the owner's benefit was allowed in the Prophet's time due to abundance, so too castration is allowed for the owner's benefit. This is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) based on 'urf and necessity. The author then cites linguistic sources (al-Sihah, al-Aini) to define terms. Then al-Muhit states the principle: causing pain to an animal for its own benefit is permissible, and there is no harm in branding for identification. However, the occupation of a human castrator (kash al-khasi min bani Adam) is disliked (makruh). The commentator then notes that the author's general mention of 'bahaim' includes horses, and then relays an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Khaniyya says horse castration is makruh, while Shams al-Aimmah (Imam al-Halwani?) says it is haram. The mufta bihi position may depend on the weight of these sources, but the text leaves it unresolved, presenting both views.

Key Terms
الموجوء (al-mawju'): The castrated animal, synonymous with al-khasi.
الخصي (al-khasi): A castrated male animal or human; also the act of castration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible (jaiz)
B) It is disliked (makruh)
C) It is forbidden (haram)
D) It is recommended (mandub)

Key Takeaway
Castration of animals is generally permissible for benefit, but horse castration is a matter of scholarly disagreement within the Hanafi school.

Rulings on Unborn Child in Distress and Pregnant Woman's Death
Addresses when to cut a fetus to save the mother, and posthumous delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for three scenarios involving an unborn child: when the child is obstructed and threatens the mother's life, when the mother dies and the child shows signs of life, and when a woman attempts abortion before the child's features are formed.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school prioritizes saving a life but forbids killing one life to save another.

This passage from al-Nawadir presents three distinct rulings regarding the unborn child. The central principle is the sanctity of life: a living child cannot be sacrificed to save the mother, but a dead mother's womb may be opened to save a living child. Abortion before the formation of limbs carries no sin. These rulings demonstrate the careful balancing of lives in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and list the three distinct situations: (1) obstructed child threatening mother's life, (2) mother dies with child moving, (3) woman trying to abort before features appear.
2
Determine the state of the child (alive/dead) in each scenario
For each case, note whether the child is alive or dead. The ruling hinges on this distinction.
In scenario 1, the child may be alive or dead; scenario 2 assumes child alive; scenario 3 is before formation.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle of life sanctity
Understand that killing a living child to save the mother is prohibited ('la yajuz'), but saving a living child by post-mortem incision is required. Abortion before features is permissible.
The principle: one life cannot be taken for another, but a dead body may be incised to save a life.
4
Note the specific details and exceptions
Observe the subsidiary rulings: incision from left side, no exhumation after burial, no sin for early abortion.
These details show the practical application of the principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النوادر : امرأة حامل اعترض الولد في بطنها ولا يمكن إلا بقطعه أرباعاً ولو لم يفعل ذلك يخاف على أمه من الموت، فإن كان الولد ميتاً في البطن فلا بأس به، وإن كان حياً لا يجوز لأن إحياء نفس بقتل نفس أخرى لم يرد في الشرع. امرأة حامل ماتت فاضطرب الولد في بطنها، فإن كان أكبر رأيه أنه حي يشق بطنها لأن ذلك تسبب في إحياء نفس محترمة بترك تعظيم الميت فالإحياء أولى ويشق بطنها من الجانب الأيسر، ولو لم يشق بطنها حتى دفنت ورؤيت في المنام أنها قالت ولدت لا ينبش القبر لأن الظاهر أنها ولدت ولداً ميتاً. امرأة عالجت في إسقاط ولدها لا تأثم ما لم يستبن شيء من خلقه.
In al-Nawadir: A pregnant woman, the child is obstructed in her womb and it is not possible except by cutting it into quarters, and if he does not do that, it is feared that the mother will die. If the child is dead in the womb, there is no harm; but if it is alive, it is not permissible because resurrecting a soul by killing another soul has not been prescribed in the Sharia. A pregnant woman died, and the child moves in her womb; if it is most likely seen that it is alive, her belly is cut open because that is a means to revive a respected soul by leaving off honoring the dead, so revival is more important; and it is cut from the left side. And if her belly was not cut until she was buried, and it was seen in a dream that she said 'I gave birth', the grave is not exhumed because the apparent is that she gave birth to a dead child. A woman who attempts to abort her child is not sinful as long as nothing of its creation is apparent.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage from al-Nawadir presents three related cases involving unborn children. First case: if a child is stuck in the birth canal and cannot be delivered except by cutting it into pieces in the womb, and the mother's life is in danger. The ruling distinguishes based on whether the child is already dead: if dead, it is permissible (la ba'sa) to cut it; if alive, it is not permissible (la yajuz) because killing one life to save another is not sanctioned in Sharia. Second case: a pregnant woman dies but the child shows signs of life (movement). If it is most likely the child is alive, the mother's belly is cut open (sheqq al-batn) from the left side to save the child, because reviving a respected life (the child) takes precedence over respecting the dead body of the mother. However, if the mother was buried without the incision, and later someone dreams that she gave birth, the grave is not exhumed because the default assumption is that the child died. Third case: a woman takes medicine or action to abort her child; she is not sinful (la ta'atham) as long as no features of a human (like limbs) have formed (istibana khalqahu). This implies that abortion before ensoulment (120 days) is less sinful, but after that it becomes prohibited.

Key Terms
اعترض الولد (i'tarada al-walad): The child is transverse/obstructed in the womb.
شق البطن (shaqq al-batn): Incision of the abdomen, performed postmortem to save a living child.
إسقاط (isqat): Abortion; attempting to terminate pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Her belly should not be cut; bury them together
B) Her belly should be cut from the right side
C) Her belly should be cut from the left side to save the child
D) The grave should be exhumed after burial

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a living child may not be sacrificed to save the mother, but a dead mother's womb may be incised to save a living child; abortion before fetal features carries no sin.

Cutting Open a Dead Body to Retrieve Property: Conflicting Rights
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cutting dead body for property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting open a dead body for property retrieval, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Muhammad and al-Jurjani, and apply the principle of weighing conflicting rights using comparative value.

Core Idea
Sanctity of the dead may be overridden by the living's property right if more valuable.

This lesson examines a delicate conflict: the inviolability of a dead human body versus the property rights of a living person. The Hanafi school addresses this through a careful weighing of rights, using analogies from everyday situations. Understanding this ruling requires appreciating how the school prioritizes haqq al-‘abd (rights of humans) over haqq Allah (rights of Allah) in certain contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two positions
Read the passage: a man swallows another's pearls or dinars, dies insolvent. Imam Muhammad says the belly is not cut open; al-Jurjani says it is cut open because the living's right takes precedence.
Note that the value is placed as a debt on the estate.
2
Understand the basis of Imam Muhammad's view
Muhammad argues that invalidating the sanctity of the dead (hurmat al-mayyit) for wealth is not permissible. This sanctity is a right of Allah or of the dead, and cannot be violated for mere property.
This is the default cautious position.
3
Understand al-Jurjani's counter-argument
Al-Jurjani prioritizes the right of the living owner (haqq al-‘abd) over the right of Allah or the dead. The living person's need justifies cutting the belly if the property is valuable.
This is based on the principle that the living's need overrides the dead's sanctity.
4
Apply the comparative value principle from analogies
The passage gives analogies (ostrich swallowing pearl, goat's horn stuck in pot, animal in house) where the more valuable item is preserved. This qiyas suggests that if the property is more valuable than the respect due to the dead, cutting may be allowed (per al-Jurjani).
The mufta bihi is not explicitly stated; the safer view is to follow Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن محمد: رجل ابتلع درة أو دنانير لآخر فمات المبتلع ولم يترك مالاً فعليه القيمة ولا يشق بطنه لأنه لا يجوز إبطال حرمة الميت لأجل الأموال، ولا كذلك المسألة المتقدمة. ونقل الجرجاني شق بطنه للحال لأن حق الآدمي مقدم على حق الله تعالى إن كان حرمة الميت حقاً لله تعالى، وإن كان حق الميت فحق الآدمي الحي مقدم على حق الميت لاحتياج الحي إلى حقه. نعامة ابتلعت لؤلؤة للغير أو دخل قرن شاة في قدر الباقلاني وتعذر إخراجه ينظر إلى أيهما أكثر قيمة فيقدم على غيره، ولذا لو دخلت دابة في دار ولا يمكن إخراجها إلا بهدم الدار ينظر إلى أيهما أكثر قيمة فيقدم على غيره فيهدم الآخر أو تذبح
And from Muhammad: A man swallowed a pearl or dinars belonging to another, and the swallower died and left no wealth; then the value is due (from his estate) and his belly is not cut open because invalidating the sanctity of the dead for the sake of wealth is not permissible; but it is not so in the previous case. Al-Jurjani reported: His belly is cut open in this case because the right of a human being takes precedence over the right of Allah if the sanctity of the dead is a right of Allah; and if it is the right of the dead, then the right of the living human takes precedence over the right of the dead due to the living person's need for his right. An ostrich swallowed a pearl belonging to another, or a goat's horn entered a pot of broad beans and its removal became difficult; consideration is given to which has more value, and the other is sacrificed. Similarly, if an animal enters a house and cannot be removed without demolishing the house, consideration is given to which is more valuable, and the other is sacrificed or demolished.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage from Imam Muhammad and later authorities deals with the conflict between preserving the sanctity of a dead body and the property rights of a living person. Two scenarios: (1) A man swallowed someone else's pearls or money and then died insolvent. According to Imam Muhammad, the value goes on his estate (does not extinguish), but the belly is not cut open to retrieve the property because that would violate the respect due to a dead human body (hurmat al-mayyit). However, this is distinct from the earlier case of a pregnant woman where the belly was cut to save a living child (as that involved saving a life). (2) A different view from al-Jurjani says the belly should be cut in this case because the right of the living person (the property owner) takes precedence. This is based on a principle: if the sanctity of the dead is considered a right of Allah (haqq Allah), then the right of the living (haqq al-‘abd) is given priority; if it is a personal right of the dead, the living person's need still takes precedence. The passage then gives analogies: if an ostrich swallows another's pearl, or a goat's horn gets stuck in a pot, the decision is based on comparative value (the more valuable item is saved, the other is sacrificed). Similarly, if an animal enters a house and cannot be removed without demolition, the more valuable is preserved. This establishes a qiyas (analogy) for the dead body case: the value of the property is weighed against the respect of the dead. The text leaves the tarjih uncertain, but the majority Hanafi view seems to follow Muhammad: do not cut the belly for property alone.

Key Terms
حرمة الميت (hurmat al-mayyit): The inviolability and respect due to a dead human body; not to be mutilated.
حق الآدمي (haqq al-ādamī): The right of a human being, especially a living person's right to property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on cutting open the belly of a dead person who swallowed another's pearls, according to Imam Muhammad?
A) It is permissible to cut the belly
B) The belly is not cut open; the value is due from the estate
C) It is obligatory to cut the belly
D) It is recommended to cut the belly

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school balances the sanctity of the dead and the property rights of the living, with Imam Muhammad defaulting to no cutting and al-Jurjani allowing it if the property is more valuable.

Permissibility of Medicinal Wine upon Physician's Prescription
Rules on medicinal use of wine based on necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which wine becomes permissible for treatment according to the Hanafi school and apply the darura principle to justify the exception.

Core Idea
Wine becomes halal for treatment when a skilled physician certifies it as the only cure, due to necessity overriding the prohibition.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with a critical exception to the general prohibition of wine. While the default ruling is that wine is haram and cannot be used for treatment, the Hanafi school recognizes that in cases of genuine medical necessity, the prohibition may be lifted. The key condition is the involvement of a skilled and trustworthy physician who confirms that the cure lies specifically in wine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The default ruling is that wine is haram and cannot be used as medicine, based on the hadith: 'Allah did not make the cure of my nation in what He has forbidden upon them.'
This is the starting point.
2
Recognize the exception condition
The text states: 'if a skilled physician informs him that the cure is in it, it is permissible'. The condition is that a tabib hadhiq (skilled physician) must attest that the cure lies specifically in wine.
The physician must be trustworthy and knowledgeable.
3
Apply the darura principle
The patient becomes like one compelled (mudtarr). Necessity (darura) permits what is normally forbidden. The hadith 'necessities permit prohibitions' (al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat) overrides the general hadith about cure not being in forbidden things.
This is a key Hanafi legal maxim.
4
Note the limitation
This permission is not absolute; it is contingent on the physician's expertise and the absence of alternative halal treatments. If a halal alternative exists, wine remains haram.
The darura must be genuine and specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التداوي بالخمر إذا أخبره طبيب حاذق أن الشفاء فيه جاز فصار حلالاً وخرج عن قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «لم يجعل الله شفاء أمتي فيما حرم عليهم لأنه صار كالمضطر.
Treating with wine: if a skilled physician informs him that the cure is in it, it is permissible and becomes halal, and it departs from his statement, peace be upon him, 'Allah did not make the cure of my nation in what He has forbidden upon them,' because he becomes like one compelled (mudtarr).
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This is a ruling on the permissibility of using wine (khamr) for medicinal purposes under certain conditions. The default ruling is that wine is haram and cannot be used for treatment, based on the hadith: 'Allah did not place the cure of my nation in what He has forbidden.' However, this ruling is an exception: if a skilled and trustworthy physician (tabib hadhiq) certifies that the cure lies specifically in wine, then it becomes permissible (ja'iz) and halal for that patient. The reason is that the patient becomes like one in a state of necessity (mudtarr), and necessity permits the forbidden (al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat). This is an application of the principle of darura in Hanafi fiqh, where the prohibition is lifted to preserve life or health.

Key Terms
المضطر (al-mudtarr): One compelled by necessity, which permits what is normally forbidden.
طبيب حاذق (tabib hadhiq): A skilled, trustworthy physician whose expertise is reliable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does wine become permissible as a treatment according to the passage?
A) If the patient desires it
B) If a skilled physician states that the cure is in wine
C) If no other medicine is available
D) If it is used externally

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits wine for medical treatment only when a skilled physician certifies its necessity, due to the overriding principle of darura.

Treatment with Permissible Bones
Ruling on treating with bones from al-Siyar al-Kabir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which animal bones are permissible for medical treatment and identify the prohibited bones according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Bones of halal animals are permitted for treatment; pig and human bones are not.

Medical treatments sometimes involve substances of animal origin. The Hanafi school provides clear guidelines on which bones may be used. This lesson extracts the ruling from Imam Muhammad's al-Siyar al-Kabir, detailing permissible and prohibited sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source and subject
The passage from al-Siyar al-Kabir addresses the permissibility of using animal bones for medical treatment. Identify the author and the core issue.
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, a leading Hanafi jurist.
2
Categorize permissible bones
List the animals mentioned: sheep, cow, camel, horse, and others. Note that 'others' implies any halal animal whose meat is eaten.
The list is not exhaustive; it covers common domesticated animals.
3
State the prohibition
Pig and human bones are explicitly prohibited for treatment. Understand the reason: pigs are impure (najis) and humans have sanctity (hurma).
Human bones are not permissible even if from a non-Muslim.
4
Note the indifference to slaughter state
Whether the animal was slaughtered (dhabih) or died naturally (mayta), and whether the bone is wet or dry, makes no difference in permissibility.
This reflects the Hanafi view that bones of halal animals are pure (tahir) even if the meat is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد في السير الكبير : لا بأس بالتداوي بالعظم إذا كان عظم شاة أو بقر أو بعير أو فرس أو غيره من الدواب إلا عظم الخنزير والآدمي فإنه لا يمكن التداوي بهما . ولا فرق فيما يجوز بين أن تكون ذكياً أو ميتاً رطباً أو يابساً. وفي الذخيرة رجل سقط سنة فأخذ سن الكلب فوضعه في موضع سنة فثبتت لا يجوز ولا يقطع، ولو أعاد سنة ثانياً وثبت قال ينظر ؛ إن كان يمكن قلع سن الكلب بغير ضرر يقلع، وإن كان لا يمكن إلا بضرر لا يقلع في التتمة يتخذ الدواء من الضفدع، ولو أكلت المرأة شيئاً لسمن نفسها لزوجها لا بأس به. وفي النوازل مرض الرجل فقال له الطبيب أخرج الدم فلم يخرجه حتى مات لا يكون مأجوراً، ولو ترك الدواء حتى مات لا يأثم. وفي الخلاصة : صام وهو غير قادر على الصيام حتى مات أثم. وفي الخانية : جامع ولم يأكل وهو قادر على الأكل كان آثماً. فرض عليه أن يأكل مقدار قوته. وفي النوازل : رجل أدخل المرارة في أصابعه للتداوي قال أبو حنيفة : يكره. وقال أبو يوسف يجوز والفقيه أبو الليث اختار قول أبي يوسف. وفي الخانية : وعلى هذا الخلاف شرب بول ما يؤكل لحمه للتداوي. وفي النوازل: العجين إذا وضع على الجرح للتداوي وعرف أن التداوي به لا بأس به. وفي السراجية : وتعليق الحجاب لا بأس به وينزعه عند الخلاء والقربان، وأفتى بعضهم بأن هذا فعل العوام والجهال . الاكتحال في يوم عاشوراء لا بأس به. ضرب الدفاف على الأبواب أيام النيروز لا يحل بل هو مكروه. وفي الغياثية : الحجامة بعد نصف الشهر حسن نافع جداً ويكره قبل
Muhammad said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: There is no harm in treating with bone if it is the bone of a sheep, cow, camel, horse, or other animals except the bone of pig and human, for it is not possible to treat with them. And there is no difference in what is permissible between whether it is slaughtered or dead, wet or dry. In al-Dhakhira: A man lost a tooth; he took a dog's tooth and placed it in the socket, and it stuck: it is not permissible and it is not removed (if removal causes harm). If he replaced it a second time and it stuck, he said: consideration is given; if it is possible to remove the dog's tooth without harm, it is removed; if it is not possible except with harm, it is not removed. In al-Tatimma: Medicine is made from frog. If a woman eats something to become fat for her husband's sake, there is no harm. In al-Nawazil: A man fell ill, and the doctor told him to let blood but he did not do so until he died; he is not rewarded (for leaving the doctor's advice). If he abandoned medicine and died, he does not sin. In al-Khulasa: He fasted while not able to fast until he died, he sinned. In al-Khaniyya: He had intercourse and did not eat while he was able to eat, he was sinful; it was obligatory upon him to eat an amount sufficient for his strength. In al-Nawazil: A man inserted bile into his fingers for treatment: Abu Hanifa said: it is disliked. Abu Yusuf said: it is permissible. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth chose the opinion of Abu Yusuf. In al-Khaniyya: and on this difference, drinking the urine of animals whose meat is eaten for treatment. In al-Nawazil: Dough placed on a wound for treatment, and it is known that treatment with it, there is no harm. In al-Sirajiyya: Hanging an amulet (hijab) there is no harm, and he removes it at the time of relieving oneself and approaching (sexual intercourse?); some muftis ruled that this is the action of the common people and ignorant. Applying kohl on the day of Ashura: there is no harm. Beating drums on doors during the days of Nayruz: not lawful, rather it is disliked. In al-Ghiyathiyya: Cupping after mid-month is good and very beneficial, and it is disliked before.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This extensive passage from multiple Hanafi sources covers various medical and health-related rulings. The first ruling from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani's al-Siyar al-Kabir states that treating with bones of halal animals (sheep, cow, camel, horse, etc.) is permissible regardless of whether the animal was slaughtered or died naturally, and whether the bone is wet or dry. However, bones of pigs and humans are not permissible to use in treatment (due to their inherent impurity or sanctity). Next, a case from al-Dhakhira: a man replaced a lost tooth with a dog's tooth that became fixed. The ruling: it is not permissible (la yajuz) to do so originally, but if it is already fixed and cannot be removed without causing harm, it is left. However, if removal is possible without harm, it should be removed. From al-Tatimma: medicine can be made from a frog (indicating permissibility of using frogs for treatment). A woman eating to become fat for her husband is permissible. In al-Nawazil: a man who neglected a doctor's advice to let blood and died is not rewarded (because he omitted a recommended treatment), but he does not sin if he simply abandoned medicine (since it is not obligatory). In al-Khulasa: fasting while unable to fast (harmfully) until death incurs sin (because of endangering oneself). In al-Khaniyya: having intercourse without eating when one is able to eat is sinful; it is obligatory (fard/fard kifayah?) to eat enough to maintain strength. Then a dispute: inserting bile into fingers for treatment: Abu Hanifa says makruh, Abu Yusuf says ja'iz; Abu al-Layth preferred Abu Yusuf's view. Similarly, drinking urine of halal animals for treatment follows the same difference. Placing dough on a wound for treatment is permissible if known to be effective. Hanging amulets (ta'widh) is permissible but should be removed during ritual impurity states like relieving oneself or sexual intercourse; some scholars consider it the action of common people. Applying kohl on Ashura is fine. Beating drums on doors during Nayruz (Persian New Year) is not halal but is makruh (disliked due to association with non-Muslim customs). Cupping (hijama) after the mid-month (i.e., after the 15th of the lunar month) is considered good and beneficial, while it is disliked before that time.

Key Terms
التداوي بالعظم (al-tadawi bi al-azm): Treating with bones; permissible for bones of halal animals, not for pig or human.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following bones is permissible for medical treatment?
A) Pig bone
B) Human bone from a deceased donor
C) Bone from a horse that died naturally
D) Bone from a cat

Key Takeaway
Bones of halal animals are permissible for medical treatment; pig and human bones are prohibited.

Dog Tooth Replacement
Ruling on dog tooth replacement from al-Dhakhira

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on using a dog's tooth as a tooth replacement and the condition for its removal.

Core Idea
Using a dog's tooth is impermissible, but if fixed and removal causes harm, it may be left.

A specific case from al-Dhakhira addresses a man who replaces a lost tooth with a dog's tooth. The ruling highlights the interaction between prohibition and harm prevention, a key Hanafi principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
A man lost a tooth and placed a dog's tooth in the socket, which became fixed. Determine the initial ruling.
The dog's tooth is considered impure (najis) or at least unlawful to use.
2
State the original ruling
The text says 'la yajuz' (it is not permissible). This is the default ruling due to the dog's impurity.
Dog is considered impure (najis al-ayn) in the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the conditional ruling
If the tooth is already fixed and cannot be removed without causing harm (dharar), it is not removed. Otherwise, it must be removed.
The principle 'la dharar wa la dirar' (no harm nor reciprocation of harm) is applied.
4
Apply the principle of necessity
The permissibility to leave it is based on the harm that would occur from removal, not on the permissibility of the original act.
This is a case of darura (necessity) overriding a prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Neglecting Medical Treatment and Obligations to Eat
Rulings on neglecting medical treatment and duties to eat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings on neglecting bloodletting, abandoning medicine, fasting while unable, and intercourse without eating.

Core Idea
Neglecting medical advice loses reward but not sin; intentionally harming oneself through fasting incurs sin.

This lesson covers several rulings from al-Nawazil, al-Khulasa, and al-Khaniyya regarding the balance between medical treatment and personal responsibility. It emphasizes the Hanafi view on when actions become sinful due to self-harm or neglect of basic needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify each case
Four separate scenarios: (a) not performing bloodletting as advised, (b) abandoning medicine entirely, (c) fasting while unable, (d) having intercourse without eating when able.
Each has a different ruling due to different levels of harm and obligation.
2
State the ruling for each
(a) Bloodletting: not rewarded (la yajur). (b) Abandoning medicine: no sin (la yathim). (c) Fasting while unable: sins (athim). (d) Intercourse without eating: sins (athim).
Reward vs. sin distinction depends on whether the action is recommended or obligatory.
3
Identify the underlying principle
Bloodletting and medicine are recommended (mandub), so omitting them loses reward but no sin. Fasting while unable and not eating after intercourse are harmful to oneself, which is prohibited (haram), thus sin.
Self-preservation (hifz al-nafs) is an obligation.
4
Distinguish between recommended and obligatory
Recommended acts: missing them loses reward but no sin. Obligatory acts: missing them incurs sin. Here, eating after intercourse is declared 'fard' (فرض عليه أن يأكل).
The text explicitly says 'farada 'alayhi an ya'kula' - it is made obligatory on him to eat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who fasts while unable (i.e., it causes severe harm) and dies?

Bile and Urine for Treatment: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on bile and urine for treatment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on using bile and urine for treatment, and state the preferred view in the school.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa disliked bile treatment; Abu Yusuf permitted it, and later Hanafis preferred permissibility.

This lesson presents an intra-madhhab dispute regarding the use of bile (marara) and urine (bol) from halal animals for medicinal purposes. The differing views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and the later preference of Abu al-Layth, show how the school resolves internal ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa said: it is disliked (makruh). Abu Yusuf said: it is permissible (ja'iz). Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi chose Abu Yusuf's view.
The dispute is about using bile from halal animals; the same difference extends to urine of halal animals.
2
Identify the source of the dispute
The issue likely revolves around whether these substances are considered impure (najis) or not. Bile is a digestive fluid, urine is a waste product. Hanafis differ on their purity.
Abu Hanifa may consider them najis or at least makruh due to being waste, while Abu Yusuf may treat them as pure within the body.
3
Note the preferred view
Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi explicitly chose (ikhtara) the opinion of Abu Yusuf, making it the mufta bihi (operative) view in the later school.
This is a common pattern: the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) often preferred the more lenient opinion when necessity or benefit is involved.
4
Extend to urine of halal animals
The text from al-Khaniyya states that the same difference applies to drinking the urine of animals whose meat is eaten (e.g., camel, cow) for treatment.
Urine of halal animals is considered pure (tahir) by some Hanafis, allowing its medicinal use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد في السير الكبير : لا بأس بالتداوي بالعظم إذا كان عظم شاة أو بقر أو بعير أو فرس أو غيره من الدواب إلا عظم الخنزير والآدمي فإنه لا يمكن التداوي بهما . ولا فرق فيما يجوز بين أن تكون ذكياً أو ميتاً رطباً أو يابساً. وفي الذخيرة رجل سقط سنة فأخذ سن الكلب فوضعه في موضع سنة فثبتت لا يجوز ولا يقطع، ولو أعاد سنة ثانياً وثبت قال ينظر ؛ إن كان يمكن قلع سن الكلب بغير ضرر يقلع، وإن كان لا يمكن إلا بضرر لا يقلع في التتمة يتخذ الدواء من الضفدع، ولو أكلت المرأة شيئاً لسمن نفسها لزوجها لا بأس به. وفي النوازل مرض الرجل فقال له الطبيب أخرج الدم فلم يخرجه حتى مات لا يكون مأجوراً، ولو ترك الدواء حتى مات لا يأثم. وفي الخلاصة : صام وهو غير قادر على الصيام حتى مات أثم. وفي الخانية : جامع ولم يأكل وهو قادر على الأكل كان آثماً. فرض عليه أن يأكل مقدار قوته. وفي النوازل : رجل أدخل المرارة في أصابعه للتداوي قال أبو حنيفة : يكره. وقال أبو يوسف يجوز والفقيه أبو الليث اختار قول أبي يوسف. وفي الخانية : وعلى هذا الخلاف شرب بول ما يؤكل لحمه للتداوي. وفي النوازل: العجين إذا وضع على الجرح للتداوي وعرف أن التداوي به لا بأس به. وفي السراجية : وتعليق الحجاب لا بأس به وينزعه عند الخلاء والقربان، وأفتى بعضهم بأن هذا فعل العوام والجهال . الاكتحال في يوم عاشوراء لا بأس به. ضرب الدفاف على الأبواب أيام النيروز لا يحل بل هو مكروه. وفي الغياثية : الحجامة بعد نصف الشهر حسن نافع جداً ويكره قبل
Muhammad said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: There is no harm in treating with bone if it is the bone of a sheep, cow, camel, horse, or other animals except the bone of pig and human, for it is not possible to treat with them. And there is no difference in what is permissible between whether it is slaughtered or dead, wet or dry. In al-Dhakhira: A man lost a tooth; he took a dog's tooth and placed it in the socket, and it stuck: it is not permissible and it is not removed (if removal causes harm). If he replaced it a second time and it stuck, he said: consideration is given; if it is possible to remove the dog's tooth without harm, it is removed; if it is not possible except with harm, it is not removed. In al-Tatimma: Medicine is made from frog. If a woman eats something to become fat for her husband's sake, there is no harm. In al-Nawazil: A man fell ill, and the doctor told him to let blood but he did not do so until he died; he is not rewarded (for leaving the doctor's advice). If he abandoned medicine and died, he does not sin. In al-Khulasa: He fasted while not able to fast until he died, he sinned. In al-Khaniyya: He had intercourse and did not eat while he was able to eat, he was sinful; it was obligatory upon him to eat an amount sufficient for his strength. In al-Nawazil: A man inserted bile into his fingers for treatment: Abu Hanifa said: it is disliked. Abu Yusuf said: it is permissible. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth chose the opinion of Abu Yusuf. In al-Khaniyya: and on this difference, drinking the urine of animals whose meat is eaten for treatment. In al-Nawazil: Dough placed on a wound for treatment, and it is known that treatment with it, there is no harm. In al-Sirajiyya: Hanging an amulet (hijab) there is no harm, and he removes it at the time of relieving oneself and approaching (sexual intercourse?); some muftis ruled that this is the action of the common people and ignorant. Applying kohl on the day of Ashura: there is no harm. Beating drums on doors during the days of Nayruz: not lawful, rather it is disliked. In al-Ghiyathiyya: Cupping after mid-month is good and very beneficial, and it is disliked before.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This extensive passage from multiple Hanafi sources covers various medical and health-related rulings. The first ruling from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani's al-Siyar al-Kabir states that treating with bones of halal animals (sheep, cow, camel, horse, etc.) is permissible regardless of whether the animal was slaughtered or died naturally, and whether the bone is wet or dry. However, bones of pigs and humans are not permissible to use in treatment (due to their inherent impurity or sanctity). Next, a case from al-Dhakhira: a man replaced a lost tooth with a dog's tooth that became fixed. The ruling: it is not permissible (la yajuz) to do so originally, but if it is already fixed and cannot be removed without causing harm, it is left. However, if removal is possible without harm, it should be removed. From al-Tatimma: medicine can be made from a frog (indicating permissibility of using frogs for treatment). A woman eating to become fat for her husband is permissible. In al-Nawazil: a man who neglected a doctor's advice to let blood and died is not rewarded (because he omitted a recommended treatment), but he does not sin if he simply abandoned medicine (since it is not obligatory). In al-Khulasa: fasting while unable to fast (harmfully) until death incurs sin (because of endangering oneself). In al-Khaniyya: having intercourse without eating when one is able to eat is sinful; it is obligatory (fard/fard kifayah?) to eat enough to maintain strength. Then a dispute: inserting bile into fingers for treatment: Abu Hanifa says makruh, Abu Yusuf says ja'iz; Abu al-Layth preferred Abu Yusuf's view. Similarly, drinking urine of halal animals for treatment follows the same difference. Placing dough on a wound for treatment is permissible if known to be effective. Hanging amulets (ta'widh) is permissible but should be removed during ritual impurity states like relieving oneself or sexual intercourse; some scholars consider it the action of common people. Applying kohl on Ashura is fine. Beating drums on doors during Nayruz (Persian New Year) is not halal but is makruh (disliked due to association with non-Muslim customs). Cupping (hijama) after the mid-month (i.e., after the 15th of the lunar month) is considered good and beneficial, while it is disliked before that time.

Key Terms
المرارة (al-marara): Bile; used in medical treatment, disputed ruling between makruh and ja'iz.
شرب البول (shurb al-bawl): Drinking urine; for halal animals, same difference as bile.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, whose opinion on bile treatment did al-Faqih Abu al-Layth choose?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Imam Muhammad
D) Al-Shafi'i

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school for using bile and urine of halal animals for treatment is ja'iz (permissible), following Abu Yusuf's view preferred by later scholars.

Miscellaneous Permissions: Frog, Dough, Amulets, Kohl, Drums, Cupping
Miscellaneous medical and ritual permissions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on using frog medicine, dough on wounds, hanging amulets, applying kohl on Ashura, beating drums during Nayruz, and cupping timings.

Core Idea
Most treatments are permissible; some have cultural or temporal restrictions like drums and cupping.

This lesson compiles several distinct rulings from al-Tatimma, al-Nawazil, al-Sirajiyya, and al-Ghiyathiyya. They cover a range of practices from medical to ritual, illustrating the breadth of Hanafi legal concern with daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify each ruling
There are six separate items: (a) making medicine from frog, (b) woman eating to become fat for husband, (c) placing dough on a wound, (d) hanging amulets (hijab), (e) applying kohl on Ashura, (f) beating drums during Nayruz, (g) cupping before and after mid-month.
Each has a specific ruling: mubah, mubah with conditions, makruh, etc.
2
Categorize by ruling
Most are mubah (permissible): frog, fattening, dough, kohl on Ashura, cupping after mid-month. Amulet is mubah with condition: remove during impurity. Drums during Nayruz are makruh (disliked). Cupping before mid-month is makruh.
The permissibility of some (like frog) may surprise those who consider it impure; Hanafis allow it.
3
Note special conditions
Amulets: must be removed at times of impurity (relieving oneself, sexual intercourse). Some scholars consider it practice of ignorant people.
This indicates a difference of opinion, but the fatwa is permissibility.
4
Contrast permissible and disliked actions
Kohl on Ashura is mubah, while beating drums during Nayruz (Persian festival) is makruh due to association with non-Muslim practices. Cupping after mid-month is beneficial, before is disliked.
Timing and cultural context affect the ruling.
