Cutting Open Deceased Pregnant Woman to Save the Fetus
Ruling on cutting open a deceased pregnant woman's abdomen when fetus is alive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting open a deceased pregnant woman's abdomen, identify the condition (physician's strong opinion), and explain the istihsan-based reasoning.

Core Idea
Saving a living fetus may override the inviolability of the deceased mother's body, based on istihsan.

In this lesson, we examine a life-and-death intersection of two sacred rights: the inviolability of the deceased (hurmah al-mayyit) and the obligation to preserve human life. The Hanafi school, through istihsan (juristic preference), permits cutting open the deceased mother’s abdomen when a physician strongly believes the fetus is alive. This ruling demonstrates how higher objectives (maqasid) can supersede secondary rulings, and it remains relevant in modern medical ethics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is cutting open the abdomen of a deceased pregnant woman. The ruling is permissible (mubah), not obligatory, because it involves mutilation of a corpse.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is istihsan (juristic preference), as the text says: 'because that is a means to revive a respected soul by leaving off respect for the dead, so revival takes precedence.'
Istihsan overrides the general prohibition against mutilating the dead.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The reasoning is that saving a living human life (ihya' nafsin muhtaramah) outweighs the duty to respect the deceased body. This is a conflict of two rights: the right of Allah in the corpse and the right of the fetus to live. The latter takes precedence.
This is a classic application of the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by their objectives).
4
Note the condition for permissibility
The permissibility is conditional on the physician's 'akbar ra'yih' (strongest opinion) that the fetus is alive. This ensures a reasonable chance of success and prevents unnecessary mutilation.
The standard is preponderant belief, not certainty.
5
Understand the exception: dream of live birth after burial
If the woman is buried without the procedure and someone sees her in a dream claiming to have given birth, the grave is not exhumed. The legal presumption (zahir) is that the child was stillborn, and dreams are not legally reliable.
This limits the ruling to before burial and shows caution against unfounded actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امرأة حامل ماتت فاضطرب الولد في بطنها، فإن كان أكبر رأيه أنه حي يشق بطنها لأن ذلك تسبب في إحياء نفس محترمة بترك تعظيم الميت فالإحياء أولى ويشق بطنها من الجانب الأيسر، ولو لم يشق بطنها حتى دفنت ورؤيت في المنام أنها قالت ولدت لا ينبش القبر لأن الظاهر أنها ولدت ولداً ميتاً.
A pregnant woman died and the fetus moved in her womb. If his strongest opinion (of the physician) is that it is alive, her belly is cut open because that is a means to revive a respected soul by leaving off respect for the dead, so revival takes precedence. Her belly is cut open from the left side. If her belly was not cut open until she was buried and she was seen in a dream saying 'I gave birth,' the grave is not opened because the apparent situation is that she gave birth to a dead child.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a medical-legal scenario: a pregnant woman dies with a living fetus. The Hanafi school, applying istihsan (juristic preference), allows cutting open her abdomen to save the fetus. The reasoning is that the inviolability of the deceased (hurmah al-mayyit) is a right of Allah, whereas preserving a living human life takes precedence over that right. The condition is that a reliable physician strongly believes the fetus is alive. The cut is made on the left side, traditionally believed to be where the fetus lies. If the woman is buried without the procedure, a reported dream does not justify exhumation because the legal presumption (zahir) is that the child was stillborn. This ruling balances respect for the dead with the obligation to save life.

Key Terms
حرمة الميت (hurmah al-mayyit): The inviolability and respect due to the deceased human body, which prohibits mutilation.
أكبر رأيه (akbar ra'yih): The strongest opinion or preponderant view of a qualified physician regarding the viability of the fetus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, when is it permissible to cut open the abdomen of a deceased pregnant woman?
A) Whenever the fetus is believed to be alive, even if the woman forbade it in her will.
B) Only if a qualified physician strongly believes the fetus is alive and will survive.
C) Always, because the fetus's life is more important than the mother's corpse.
D) Only if the woman dies during labor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits cutting open a deceased pregnant woman to save a viable fetus, applying istihsan to prioritize life over the inviolability of the dead, provided a physician's strong opinion indicates fetal life.

The Hanafi Ruling on Abortion Before Fetal Features Appear
Ruling on abortion before fetal features

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on abortion before fetal formation and identify the condition (istabāna khalquh) that changes the ruling.

Core Idea
Before distinct features appear, no sin; after that, abortion becomes sinful.

This brief passage from the Hanafi text presents a clear-cut ruling on attempted abortion. Unlike many modern discussions that focus on ensoulment at 120 days, the Hanafi school uses a different benchmark: the appearance of discernible human features. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the school's nuanced approach to fetal life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a woman attempting to abort her own child. The ruling given is that she does not incur sin (la ta'tham).
2
Note the qualifying condition
The absence of sin is conditional: 'as long as nothing of his creation has become apparent' (ma lam yastabin shay' min khalqih).
The key term is istabāna khalquh – the stage when the fetus's limbs and features become distinct.
3
Understand the implication of the condition
Once features become apparent, the ruling changes: the act becomes sinful. The condition acts as a clear legal marker.
The text does not explicitly state the post-formation ruling, but it is inferred logically.
4
Recognize the underlying rationale
The appearance of human features indicates the beginning of legal protection for the fetus. Before that, the fetus is not yet considered a fully protected human life.
This is consistent with the hadith on ensoulment at 120 days, but the ruling uses a visible feature marker.
5
Distinguish between 'no sin' and 'permissible'
The text only addresses sin; it does not declare the act mubah (permissible). Classical Hanafi scholars often consider it makruh tanzihi (disliked) even before features appear, unless there is a legitimate need.
This nuance is important to avoid oversimplification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امرأة عالجت في إسقاط ولدها لا تأثم ما لم يستبن شيء من خلقه.
A woman who treats (i.e., attempts) to abort her child does not sin as long as nothing of his creation (features/limbs) has become apparent.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This is a brief ruling from the Hanafi school on attempted abortion. The key point is that before the fetus has developed any discernible human features (e.g., limbs, fingers), the woman does not incur sin (ithm) for taking measures to induce abortion. The underlying reasoning is that before ensoulment (which traditionally occurs at 120 days according to a hadith), the fetus is not yet a fully protected human life. However, this does not mean it is encouraged; it only means there is no sin. After features become apparent, abortion would be sinful (haram) because it destroys a potential life. This ruling should be understood in the context of classical Hanafi fiqh and not as a modern permissive stance.

Key Terms
استبان خلقه (istabāna khalquh): The stage when the fetus's limbs and features become distinct, typically after 40 days or more in classical jurisprudence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a woman become sinful for attempting abortion according to this ruling?
A) As soon as she takes any abortive action.
B) Only after the fetus has developed discernible human features.
C) After 40 days of pregnancy.
D) At the moment of conception.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the prohibition and sin of abortion are tied to the appearance of the fetus's features, not to a fixed day count.

6 min
Swallowing Another's Property: Inviolability vs. Owner's Right
Ruling on swallowing another's property and the inviolability of the deceased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting open a deceased person who swallowed another's property and explain the legal reasoning behind the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
A deceased's inviolability is not sacrificed for property, but the owner's right may compete.

This lesson examines a specific case where a person swallows another's valuable (pearl or dinars) and then dies. Hanafi jurists debate whether the stomach may be cut open to retrieve the property. The ruling turns on the weight given to the inviolability of the dead (hurmah al-mayyit) versus the living owner's right to property. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
A person swallows another's pearl or dinars, then dies leaving no other assets.
The property belongs to another living person.
2
State Imam Muhammad's ruling
Imam Muhammad rules: do not cut open the stomach; instead, the value is owed from the estate.
The inviolability of the dead takes precedence over property.
3
State al-Jurjani's ruling
Al-Jurjani narrates: cut open the stomach to retrieve the property immediately.
The living owner's right prevails over the deceased's inviolability.
4
Compare legal reasoning
Imam Muhammad argues that annulling the inviolability of the dead for wealth is not permissible. Al-Jurjani argues that the living owner's need for his property outweighs the dead's right.
Both rely on different prioritizations of rights.
5
Determine thamarah al-khilaf
If the stomach is cut, the property is retrieved and given to the owner. If not, the owner gets the value (if estate has assets) but the body is preserved.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is Imam Muhammad's, as per later works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن محمد: رجل ابتلع درة أو دنانير لآخر فمات المبتلع ولم يترك مالاً فعليه القيمة ولا يشق بطنه لأنه لا يجوز إبطال حرمة الميت لأجل الأموال، ولا كذلك المسألة المتقدمة. ونقل الجرجاني شق بطنه للحال لأن حق الآدمي مقدم على حق الله تعالى إن كان حرمة الميت حقاً لله تعالى، وإن كان حق الميت فحق الآدمي الحي مقدم على حق الميت لاحتياج الحي إلى حقه.
And from Muhammad: A man swallowed a pearl or dinars belonging to another, then the swallower died and left no wealth. Upon him is the value, and his belly is not cut open because it is not permissible to annul the inviolability of the dead for the sake of wealth. But the previous case is not like this. Al-Jurjani narrated: cut open his belly in the scenario because the right of a human being is given precedence over the right of Allah if the inviolability of the dead is a right of Allah, and if it is a right of the dead, then the right of the living human takes precedence over the right of the dead due to the living person's need for his right.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage discusses a case where a person swallows another's property (a pearl or dinars) and then dies without leaving any other assets. The Hanafi ruling from Imam Muhammad is that the swallower's estate owes the value (qīmah) to the owner, but the body of the deceased is not cut open to retrieve the items. The reasoning is that the inviolability of the deceased (hurmah al-mayyit) cannot be violated for mere property rights. This contrasts with the earlier case of the pregnant woman where the inviolability was set aside to save a life. However, a narration from al-Jurjani suggests that the belly should be cut open in this case because the right of the living owner (a human right) takes precedence over the right of Allah (the inviolability of the dead) or over the right of the deceased. The text does not resolve which view is relied upon. The following lines extend the principle to similar cases: an ostrich swallowing a pearl, a goat's horn stuck in a pot, etc., where the more valuable item is preserved by sacrificing the other.

Key Terms
حرمة الميت (hurmah al-mayyit): The inviolability of the deceased person's body, prohibiting mutilation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling attributed to Imam Muhammad, what is the outcome when a person dies after swallowing another's property and leaves no wealth?
A) The belly is cut open to retrieve the property.
B) The value of the property is due from the estate, but the belly is not cut open.
C) The owner has no claim because the swallower died.
D) The belly is cut open only if the owner demands it.

Key Takeaway
The inviolability of the dead is given priority over property rights, so the stomach is not cut open for retrieval; the owner instead receives compensation from the estate if possible.

Hanafi Ruling on Quran Recitation at Graves
Compiles rulings on recitation at graves from multiple later Hanafi sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Quran recitation at graves and distinguish between concealed and aloud recitation, including the exception for Surah al-Mulk.

Core Idea
Recitation at graves is mubah when concealed; aloud is disliked except for Surah al-Mulk.

This passage from later Hanafi works compiles rulings on reciting Quran at graves. It distinguishes between concealed and loud recitation, and notes a special allowance for Surah al-Mulk due to specific reports. The Hanafi position balances permissibility with adab, emphasizing sincerity over ostentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? (Recitation of Quran at graves.) What is the default hukm? (It depends on how it is done.)
2
Distinguish concealed vs aloud
If recited concealed (sirran), it is not disliked (la yukrah). If recited aloud (jaharan), it is disliked (yukrah).
Concealment here means low voice, not hidden from people.
3
Note the exception for Surah al-Mulk
Shaykh Muhammad ibn Ibrahim permits reciting Surah al-Mulk aloud or concealed without dislike due to specific reports (athar).
Other surahs should not be recited aloud.
4
Consider the virtue of Surah al-Ikhlas
Reciting Surat al-Ikhlas seven times brings forgiveness: if the deceased was not forgiven, he is forgiven; if already forgiven, the reciter's sins are gifted to the deceased.
This is a recommended practice, not obligatory.
5
Evaluate hiring a reader
Some disliked hiring a reader, but the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that it is not disliked, and the deceased benefits from the recitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النوازل : قراءة القرآن عند المقابر إذا أخفاها لا يكره. وإن جهر بها يكره، والشيخ محمد بن إبراهيم قال : لا بأس أن يقرأ سورة الملك على المقابر سواء أخفاها أو جهر بها، أما غيرها فلا يقرؤها لورود الآثار بسورة الملك. وعن أبي بكر وابن أبي سعيد: يستحب العز من عرشك وبحق فلان واللعب بالشطرنج والنرد وكل لهو وجعل الراية في عنق زيادة القبر وقراءة سورة الإخلاص سبع مرات، فإن كان الميت غير مغفور له غفر له، وإن كان مغفوراً له غفر لهذا القارئ ووهبت ذنوبه للميت. وفي التتارخانية : رجل مات فأجلس وارثه رجلاً على قبره يقرأ القرآن قال بعضهم يكره، والمختار أنه لا يكره والأشبه أنه ينتفع الميت.
In al-Nawazil: reciting Quran at graves is not disliked if concealed, but if aloud it is disliked. Shaykh Muhammad ibn Ibrahim said: there is no harm in reciting Surah al-Mulk at graves whether concealed or aloud, but other surahs should not be recited because of the reports concerning Surah al-Mulk. From Abu Bakr and Ibn Abi Sa'id: it is recommended... and reciting Surah al-Ikhlas seven times: if the deceased was not forgiven, he is forgiven; if he was forgiven, the reciter is forgiven and his sins are gifted to the deceased. In al-Tatar Khaniyah: a man dies and his heir seats a person at his grave to recite Quran; some said it is disliked, but the chosen view is that it is not disliked, and it is more likely that the deceased benefits.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage compiles several rulings on Quran recitation at graves. The Hanafi position distinguishes between concealed (not disliked) and aloud (disliked). However, Shaykh Muhammad ibn Ibrahim permits Surah al-Mulk in either case due to specific reports. Reciting Surah al-Ikhlas seven times has special virtue: if the deceased was sinful, he is forgiven; if righteous, the reciter is forgiven and sins are transferred to the deceased. Hiring a reader at the grave is not disliked; the deceased benefits. The passage reflects the Hanafi emphasis on ikhlas (sincerity) and istihsan (juristic preference) in matters of benefit to the dead.

Key Terms
المقابر (al-maqābir): Graveyards, the place of burial.
النوازل (al-nawāzil): A collection of later Hanafi fatwas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Surah al-Fatihah
B) Surah al-Mulk
C) Surah al-Ikhlas
D) Surah Ya-Sin

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits Quran recitation at graves when done quietly, and restricts loud recitation except for Surah al-Mulk due to specific prophetic reports.

The Hanafi Ruling on Preferring Some Surahs Over Others
Discusses differing views on the virtue of certain surahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on tafdil and evaluate the reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
All Quran is Allah's word; preferring one part over another may lead to disrespect.

This passage from al-Ghiyathiyyah addresses a subtle but important question: can we say that some surahs are 'better' than others? While hadith mention special virtues, the Hanafi school advises caution to avoid diminishing any part of the Quran. We'll explore the two interpretations of 'better' and derive the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage deals with tafdil—declaring some surahs or verses superior to others. Although reports indicate special virtues (like for Ayat al-Kursi), the meaning of 'superior' is disputed.
2
Analyze the two interpretations
Some say 'better' means greater reward; others say it means more awakening to the heart. The author considers the second interpretation closer to correctness.
3
Extract the mufta bihi ruling
Despite acknowledging virtues, the best course is not to verbally prefer some parts of the Quran over others. This avoids any implication that other parts are less valuable.
4
Compare with cross-madhhab view
Other schools, particularly the Shafi'i, often emphasize specific virtues. The Hanafi position is more cautious, prioritizing reverence for the entire Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الغياثية : وردت الأخبار بتفضيل بعض السور والآيات على بعض كآية الكرسي ونحوها واختلفوا في معنى الأفضل ؛ قال بعض : إن ثواب قراءتها أفضل. وقيل بأنها للقلب أيقظ وهذا أقرب إلى الصواب والأفضل أن لا يفضل بعض القرآن على بعض.
In al-Ghiyathiyyah: Reports have come about the superiority of some surahs and verses over others, like Ayat al-Kursi and similar. They differed concerning the meaning of 'superior'. Some said: the reward of reciting them is better. It is also said that they are more awakening to the heart, and this is closer to correctness. The best is not to prefer some parts of the Quran over others.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage from al-Ghiyathiyyah (a Hanafi work) addresses the issue of tafdil (preferring) some Quranic verses over others. While hadith indicate special virtues for certain surahs, the author notes two interpretations: one says the reward is greater; the other says they are more effective in awakening the heart. The latter is deemed closer to correct. Ultimately, the best approach is not to make such comparisons, as all of the Quran is the word of Allah. This reflects the Hanafi concern for avoiding any potential disrespect.

Key Terms
تفضيل (tafḍīl): Declaring one thing superior to another.
آية الكرسي (āyat al-kursī): Verse 255 of Surah al-Baqarah, considered the greatest verse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That their reward is greater
B) That they are more awakening to the heart
C) That they are shorter
D) That they have more letters

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that while some Quranic portions have special virtues, it is best not to verbally prefer one over another to maintain reverence for the entire Quran.

Writing Quran for Healing: Permissibility of Using Blood
Permits writing Fatihah with blood for medical treatment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on writing al-Fatihah with blood for medical treatment and distinguish it from using urine.

Core Idea
Writing Quran with blood for cure is permissible if done with sincere intention and no shirk.

This lesson examines a rare but important ruling from al-Tatar Khaniyah: the permissibility of writing al-Fatihah with one's own blood on the afflicted area for healing. While unusual, this falls under the broader Hanafi principle of allowing Quranic ruqyah for genuine medical needs. The key distinction is between using blood, which is ritually impure but necessary, and using urine, which is not established from the early scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is writing al-Fatihah with blood on the mouth/face for medical treatment. The ruling is mubah (permissible).
2
Identify the condition
The permissibility is conditioned on the act being for istishfa' (seeking cure) and mu'alajah (treatment).
3
Note the source authority
The ruling is from al-Tatar Khaniyah, a later Hanafi fatwa collection (fatawa mutakhkhirin), not from Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Distinguish between blood and urine
Blood is specifically permitted in this context; urine has no precedent from early scholars, though a minority opinion allows it if proven effective.
5
Recognize the broader principle
This ruling reflects the Hanafi leniency in allowing ruqyah with Quranic words for healing, provided no shirk is involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية : وإذا سال الدم من الأنف فكتب الفاتحة بالدم على الفم والوجه جاز للاستشفاء والمعالجة، ولو أراد أن يكتب ذلك بالبول لم ينقل ذلك عن المتقدمين، وقيل لا بأس به إذا علم به الشفاء.
In al-Tatar Khaniyah: if blood flows from the nose and one writes al-Fatihah with that blood on the mouth and face, it is permitted for seeking cure and treatment. If one wants to write it with urine, that has not been transmitted from the early scholars, and it is said there is no harm in it if healing is known from it.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage discusses using Quranic verses for medical treatment (ruqyah). Writing al-Fatihah with blood on the affected area is permitted as a means of healing. The permissibility is based on istihsan and the principle that necessity allows unusual means. However, using urine is not reported from the salaf, and the author notes a minority view permitting it only if empirically effective. The Hanafi position generally permits such treatment if it involves no shirk and is done with the intention of seeking Allah's cure through His words.

Key Terms
الاستشفاء (al-istishfā'): Seeking cure (through Quranic recitation or writing).

Hanafi Prohibition of Chess, Backgammon, and All Frivolous Games
Prohibits playing chess, backgammon, and all frivolous games

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on playing chess, backgammon, and frivolous games, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Chess, backgammon, and all frivolous games are absolutely prohibited in the Hanafi school.

This lesson unpacks a concise yet pivotal statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: playing chess, backgammon, and any form of lahw (amusement) is not permissible. While the matn is brief, its implications are broad, covering all activities that distract from the remembrance of Allah. We will explore the ruling's legal weight, its basis in Hanafi usul, and how it contrasts with positions in other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
Spot the specific activities mentioned: chess (al-shiṭranj), backgammon (al-nard), and the general category 'all frivolous games' (kullu lahw). The term 'lahw' is key—it encompasses any amusement that diverts from religious obligations.
The Arabic includes 'kullu lahw' which broadens the scope beyond board games.
2
Extract the Ruling
The phrase 'la yajūzu dhālika' means 'that is not permissible.' In fiqh terminology, 'la yajūzu' indicates a prohibition (haram or makruh tahrimi). The Hanafi position, as per the matn, treats it as strictly prohibited (haram).
Some later Hanafis distinguished between chess with and without stakes, but the relied-upon view is absolute prohibition.
3
Classify the Hukm Level
Determine the precise legal category. According to the Zahir al-Riwayah and the mufta bihi, playing these games is haram. This is because they involve 'lahw' and can lead to neglect of prayers and other duties.
The Shafi'i school rules chess as halal if free from gambling and not causing neglect; the Hanafi view is stricter.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Some early Hanafis (e.g., Imam Muhammad) reportedly allowed chess without stakes, but the majority and the matn's author prohibit it absolutely. The mufta bihi follows the prohibition to avoid opening the door to frivolity.
This ikhtilaf is mentioned in the commentary; the matn settles on the stricter view.
5
Consider Practical Application
Think about modern scenarios: online chess or mobile games that consume time and lead to neglect of prayers. The ruling applies regardless of stakes, as the core issue is the distracted amusement (lahw) itself.
The ruling is not limited to board games; video games and time-wasting hobbies may also fall under 'kullu lahw' if they lead to similar harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( واللعب بالشطرنج والنرد وكل لهو) يعني لا يجوز ذلك
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And playing chess, backgammon, and all frivolous games) meaning that is not permissible.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This is a brief commentary on the matn phrase from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author asserts that playing chess, backgammon (nard), and any lahw (amusement) is impermissible. The Hanafi position is that such games are either haram or makruh tahrimi depending on whether they distract from obligations or involve gambling. Chess is particularly problematic if played for stakes or if it leads to neglect of prayers. This ruling reflects the general Hanafi principle that anything that distracts from the remembrance of Allah is prohibited.

Key Terms
الشطرنج (al-shiṭranj): Chess; a board game.
النرد (al-nard): Backgammon; a dice game.
لهو (lahw): Frivolous amusement that distracts from remembrance of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Disliked but not haram
C) Not permissible
D) Recommended if for skill

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school absolutely prohibits playing chess, backgammon, and any frivolous amusement that distracts from the remembrance of Allah, regardless of stakes.

Types of Du'a and Corresponding Hand Gestures
Classifies du'a into four types with specific hand gestures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of du'a according to Hanafi jurists and state the recommended hand position for each type.

Core Idea
Du'a has four forms; each form carries a distinct posture aligning outward gesture with inward state.

Having understood that du'a is an act of worship, we now explore its physical expressions. The Hanafi school, drawing from transmitted practice, classifies supplication into four categories based on the supplicant's emotional state and the nature of the request. Each category has a specific hand position taught by the Prophet ﷺ, as recorded by Ibn al-Hanafiyyah and cited by al-Sighnaqi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List the four types of du'a mentioned in the text: du'a al-raghbah (desire), du'a al-rahbah (fear), du'a al-tadarru' (humility), and du'a al-khafiyah (secrecy).
Each type corresponds to a different emotional state.
2
Match each category with its description
Understand the context: raghbah is for seeking something, rahbah is for seeking protection, tadarru' is for earnest supplication, khafiyah is for private devotion.
The emotional tone dictates the outward gesture.
3
Learn the hand gesture for each
Memorize the positions: raghbah (palms up), rahbah (palms back to face), tadarru' (specific finger arrangement), khafiyah (no external gesture).
These gestures are sunnah, not obligatory.
4
Apply the correct gesture in practice
Consider the situation: when expressing need, use raghbah; when fearing something, use rahbah; when entreating, use tadarru'; when supplicating silently, no gesture.
This helps align the body with the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال السغناقي ابن الحنفية قال : الدعاء أربعة : دعاء رغبة، ودعاء رهبة، ودعاء تضرع، ودعاء خفية. ففي دعاء الرغبة يجعل بطون كفيه إلى السماء، وفي دعاء الرهبة يجعل ظهورها إلى وجهه كالمستغيث من الشيء، وفي دعاء التضرع يعقد الخنصر والبنصر ويحلق الإبهام والوسطى ويشير بالسبابة، وفي دعاء الخفية يفعل ما يفعل المرء في نفسه.
Al-Sighnaqi Ibn al-Hanafiyyah said: Supplication is four kinds: supplication of desire, supplication of fear, supplication of humility, and supplication of secrecy. In the supplication of desire, one turns the palms of his hands towards the sky; in the supplication of fear, he turns the backs towards his face like one seeking refuge from something; in the supplication of humility, he folds his little finger and ring finger, forms a ring with thumb and middle finger, and points with the index finger; in the supplication of secrecy, he does what one does within himself.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage from al-Sighnaqi explains the etiquettes of du'a based on the type of request. Each posture reflects the psychological state: desire (rghabah) looks upward with open palms; fear (rahbah) shows palms outward in supplication; humility (tadarru') uses a specific finger arrangement indicating earnestness; secrecy (khafiyah) is inward, without physical gesture. These distinctions are practical guidance for the supplicant to align his outward state with his inner feeling.

Key Terms
دعاء الرغبة (du'ā' al-raghbah): Supplication expressing desire for something.
دعاء الرهبة (du'ā' al-rahbah): Supplication expressing fear of something.
دعاء التضرع (du'ā' al-taḍarru'): Supplication with humility and earnestness.
دعاء الخفية (du'ā' al-khafiyah): Silent, inward supplication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Palms toward sky
B) Backs of palms toward face
C) With folded fingers pointing
D) No specific position

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes four du'a postures, each reflecting the emotional tone of the supplication – desire, fear, humility, or secrecy.

Recommended Phrasing for Seeking Forgiveness
Distinguishes between two phrases for seeking forgiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi recommended phrase for seeking forgiveness and explain why it is preferred.

Core Idea
When seeking forgiveness, use humble words that ask for repentance, not assert it.

This passage from al-Tatimma highlights a subtle point of adab (etiquette) regarding how we phrase our repentance. While both phrases are permissible, the Hanafi school encourages humility by asking Allah for the ability to repent rather than claiming repentance outright.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrases
Locate the two Arabic phrases: 'astaghfirullāha wa atūbu ilayh' and 'astaghfirullāha wa as'aluhu al-tawbah'. Note their literal meanings.
2
Understand the preference
Al-Tatimma recommends against the first phrase because asserting 'I repent' may imply self-praise. The second phrase humbly requests the ability to repent from Allah. Recognize that al-Tahawi permits both, but the preferred view is to use the second.
This is a matter of adab (etiquette), not obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتمة : لا يقول الرجل استغفر الله وأتوب إليه ولكن يقول استغفر الله وأسأله التوبة. قال أبو جعفر الطحاوي : لا بأس به.
In al-Tatimma: A man should not say 'I seek forgiveness of Allah and I repent to Him' but should say 'I seek forgiveness of Allah and I ask Him for repentance.' Abu Ja'far al-Tahawi said: There is no harm in it.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage from al-Tatimma (a Hanafi work) prescribes a specific wording for seeking forgiveness. Saying 'I seek forgiveness and I repent' (astaghfirullah wa atubu ilayh) implies asserting one's own repentance, which may be seen as self-praise. The recommended phrase is 'I seek forgiveness and I ask Him for repentance' (astaghfirullah wa as'aluhu al-tawbah), which humbly requests the ability to repent. However, al-Tahawi considers both permissible. The Hanafi position leans toward the more humble phrasing as better, but not obligatory.

Key Terms
استغفر الله وأتوب إليه (astaghfirullāha wa atūbu ilayh): A phrase meaning 'I seek forgiveness of Allah and I repent to Him'.
استغفر الله وأسأله التوبة (astaghfirullāha wa as'aluhu al-tawbah): A phrase meaning 'I seek forgiveness of Allah and I ask Him for repentance'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Tatimma, which phrase is recommended when seeking forgiveness?
A) أستغفر الله وأتوب إليه
B) أستغفر الله وأسأله التوبة
C) Both are equally recommended
D) Neither is correct

Key Takeaway
Though both phrases are permissible, the Hanafi school recommends the humble wording 'Astaghfirullah wa as'aluhu al-tawbah'.

Prohibition of Invoking Allah by the Right of a Creature
Explaining the prohibition of invoking Allah by the right of a creature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on saying 'bi-haqqi fulan' in supplication and explain the theological rationale for its prohibition.

Core Idea
Invoking Allah by the right of a creature is prohibited because no creature has a right over the Creator.

This lesson addresses a common supplicatory phrase that raises a theological concern. While some may think invoking by the right of prophets or saints is a sign of reverence, the Hanafi school deems it impermissible due to the implications for tawhid. We will examine the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its ruling
The phrase 'bi-haqqi fulan' (by the right of so-and-so) and similar invocations by the right of prophets, saints, or sacred places are ruled impermissible (haram).
The text explicitly says 'la yajuz' (it is not permissible).
2
Understand the theological basis
No creature has a right over Allah; He is not obligated to respond to anyone. Saying 'by the right of so-and-so' implies an obligation upon Allah, which contradicts His sovereignty.
This is based on the principle that Allah bestows mercy by His will, not due to any claim.
3
Distinguish from permissible phrases
Saying 'bi-haqqi Allah' (by the right of Allah) or 'billah' (by Allah) to another person is not legally obligatory but recommended. This is not the same as invoking Allah by the right of a creature.
The text provides this as a distinct ruling.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Al-Tatar Khaniyya reports traditions that indicate permissibility, but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is prohibition as stated by the author.
The difference shows that there is some internal discussion, but the established view is prohibition.
5
Apply the ruling
Any supplication that uses 'bi-haqqi' followed by a creature is impermissible. Permissible alternatives include invoking by Allah's attributes, names, or mercy.
For example, say 'O Allah, by Your mercy, forgive me' instead of 'by the right of the Prophet'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وبحق فلان) يعني لا يجوز أن يقول بحق فلان عليك وكذا بحق أنبيائك وأوليائك ورسلك والبيت والمشعر الحرام لأنه لا حق للمخلوق على الخالق وإنما يخص برحمته من يشاء من غير وجوب عليه ولو قال رجل لغيره بحق الله أو بالله افعل كذا لا يجب عليه أن يأتي بذلك شرعاً ويستحب أن يأتي بذلك. وفي التتارخانية : وجاء في الآثار ما يدل على جواز ذلك .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And by right of so-and-so) meaning it is not permissible to say 'by the right of so-and-so upon You' nor 'by the right of Your prophets, Your saints, Your messengers, the House, and the Sacred Monument' because there is no right of the created over the Creator. He only bestows His mercy upon whom He wills without any obligation upon Him. And if a man says to another 'by the right of Allah' or 'by Allah, do such and such,' it is not obligatory upon him to do it legally, but it is recommended that he do it. And in al-Tatar Khaniyya: And there has come in the traditions what indicates the permissibility of that.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the sharh of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the issue of invoking Allah by the right of a created being, such as a prophet or saint. The author, Ibn Nujaym, explains that this is not permissible because no creature has a right over Allah; He is not obligated to respond to anyone. The phrase 'bi-haqqi fulaan' implies that the creature has a claim on Allah, which is incorrect. The ruling extends to saying 'bi-haqqi anbiya'ika' (by the right of Your prophets) or 'bi-haqqi baytika' (by the right of Your House) etc. However, if one says 'bi-haqqi Allah' (by the right of Allah) or 'billah' (by Allah) asking someone to do something, it is not legally obligatory for the person to comply, but it is recommended to do so. A different view is reported from al-Tatar Khaniyya that indicates permissibility based on some traditions, but the relied-upon position remains that it is impermissible. The Hanafi school maintains that supplication should be made with humility and recognition of Allah's sovereignty, not by claiming rights of others.

Key Terms
بحق فلان (bi-haqqi fulaan): A supplicatory phrase invoking Allah by the right or merit of a specific person, which is considered impermissible because it implies a claim upon Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on saying 'bi-haqqi fulaan' (by the right of so-and-so) in supplication?
A) Permissible (ja'iz)
B) Recommended (mandub)
C) Prohibited (haram)
D) Obligatory (wajib)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits invoking Allah by the right of any creature because it implies an obligation upon Allah, which contradicts His sovereignty.

Ruling on Playing Chess, Backgammon, and Idle Diversions
Prohibition of idle games with evidence and response to opposing view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on chess, backgammon, and other idle diversions, cite the primary hadith evidence, explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i position, and identify the exceptions for playing duff at weddings and for children.

Core Idea
All idle play is haram except three; chess and backgammon are prohibited, but the duff for weddings and children is permitted.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on idle games and sounds. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the prohibition of chess and backgammon, citing a key hadith. He then addresses the Shafi'i permissibility of chess and adds rulings from al-Muhit on the permissibility of the duff in specific contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling for chess/backgammon
The act is playing chess, backgammon, and any idle diversion. The ruling is that it is not permissible (la yajuz), i.e., haram, based on the general hadith.
The prohibition covers all idle play unless specifically exempted.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'Every idle play of the son of Adam is haram except three: a man playing with his wife, training his horse, and competing with his bow.' This hadith is narrated by several compilers.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear and definitive regarding prohibition.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i permits chess without gambling and without neglecting obligations, arguing that it sharpens the intellect. The Hanafi school disagrees.
The dispute is over whether the general prohibition covers chess or not.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the general hadith covers all idle play, and chess is not among the exceptions. Other hadiths on this topic are numerous and well-known, so the prohibition stands.
The Hanafi position is considered mufta bihi.
5
Apply rulings on idle sounds and duff
From al-Muhit, it is makruh to play such games because they are the play of the Jews. Listening to idle sounds is also prohibited (makruh tahrimi). The duff (tambourine) is permitted at weddings and for a child without sin, as per Abu Yusuf.
These are separate but related rulings on idle entertainment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( واللعب بالشطرنج والنرد وكل لهو) يعني لا يجوز ذلك لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام كل لعب ابن آدم حرام إلا ثلاثاً ملاعبة الرجل أهله وتأديبه لفرسه ومناضلته لقوسه» وأباح الشافعي الشطرنج من غير قمار ولا إخلال بالواجبات لأنه يذكي الأفهام والحجة عليه ما روينا والأحاديث الواردة في ذلك هي كثيرة شهيرة فتركنا ذكرها لشهرتها. وفي المحيط : ويكره اللعب بالشطرنج والنرد والأربعة عشر لأنها لعب اليهود، ويكره استماع صوت اللهو والضرب به، والوجب على الإنسان أن يجتهد ما أمكن حتى لا يسمع، ولا بأس بضرب الدف في العرس وسئل أبو يوسف عن الدف في غير العرس بأن تضرب المرأة في غير فسق للصبي قال : لا بأس بذلك.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And playing chess, backgammon, and every idle diversion) means that is not permissible. Because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'Every idle play of the son of Adam is forbidden except three: a man playing with his wife, training his horse, and competing with his bow.' Al-Shafi'i permitted chess without gambling and without neglect of obligations, because it sharpens the intellect. The proof against him is what we have narrated. The hadiths on this matter are many and famous, so we have omitted mentioning them due to their fame. And in al-Muhit: It is disliked to play chess, backgammon, and the fourteen, because they are the play of the Jews. It is disliked to listen to idle sounds and beat them. It is obligatory for a person to strive as much as possible not to hear them. There is no harm in beating the duff at a wedding. And Abu Yusuf was asked about the duff at other than a wedding, where a woman beats it without sin for a child: he said there is no harm in that.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, followed by an inter-madhhab dispute. The author comments on the matn item 'al-la'bu bi al-shatranj wa al-nard wa kulli lahw' (playing chess, backgammon, and every idle diversion). He states that it is not permissible, citing a well-known hadith that declares all idle play of humans haram except three categories: a man playing with his wife (intimate play), training his horse (for jihad or travel), and archery (for combat skill). All other forms of idle play fall under the general prohibition. Imam al-Shafi'i allowed chess specifically, provided it does not involve gambling or cause neglect of religious duties, arguing that it sharpens the mind. The Hanafi response is based on the general hadith and other specific traditions which are too numerous to mention. The commentary adds from al-Muhit that such games are disliked (makruh) because they are associated with the practices of Jews. It also rules that it is obligatory to avoid listening to idle sounds (lahw) and beating instruments. The duff (tambourine) is excepted at weddings, and Abu Yusuf also permitted it for a child's entertainment as long as it does not involve sin.

Key Terms
شطرنج (shatranj): A board game played with pieces; considered a form of idle diversion (lahw) and prohibited in the Hanafi school.
نرد (nard): Backgammon; also considered a game of chance and prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'Every idle play of the son of Adam is haram except three...'
B) 'Chess is a game of the devil'
C) 'Whoever plays backgammon has disobeyed Allah and His Messenger'
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
All idle play is prohibited in the Hanafi school except for three specific categories; chess and backgammon are included in the prohibition.

Responding to Salam: Obligation and Best Practice
Rulings on responding to salam and its preferred form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on responding to salam and explain the best grammatical form of the response.

Core Idea
Responding to salam is wajib, and the best form includes 'wa' and additional blessings.

The greeting of salam is a key social obligation in Islam. This lesson focuses on the mandatory nature of the response and the ideal wording, as well as related etiquettes such as using the plural form and the sufficiency of one response from a group.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hukm of responding to salam
Locate the clear statement that 'radd al-salam wajib' and note its binding nature.
Hanafi: wajib, not fard, due to zanni evidence.
2
Determine which is better: initiator or responder
Read the ikhtilaf and the conclusion that the responder has more reward.
The text states 'al-radd aktharu ajran'.
3
Extract the best wording of the response
Note the recommendation to include 'wa' and add 'rahmatullahi wa barakatuh'.
Best: 'wa alaykum al-salam wa rahmatullahi wa barakatuh'.
4
Apply the ruling to group scenarios
Understand that one response from the group suffices for all.
Also note the use of plural form even for a single addressee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة : لا بأس بالغناء في الأعياد. وفي السراجية : وقراءة الأشعار إذا لم يكن فيه ذكر الفسق والغلام لا يكره. وفي الكافي : مستأجر الدار إذا ظهر منه الفسق بأن يجمع الناس على شرب الخمر يمنع فإذا لم يمتنع يخرج. ولم ير الإمام رحمه الله بالسلام عليه بأساً ليشغله عما هو فيه وكره أبو يوسف السلام تحقيراً له اهـ . رجل يدعوه الأمير فيسأله عن أشياء فيتكلم بما يوافق الحق يناله منه المكروه لا ينبغي له أن يتكلم إلا بالحق إلا أن يخاف القتل أو إتلاف عضو وأن يأخذ ماله. ولو مر على قوم وفيهم أهل الذمة أو كافر قال بعضهم يقول السلام على من ابتع الهدى، والصحيح أنه يقول السلام عليكم وينوي المسلمين في قلبه وفي التتارخانية : إذا استقبل المسلم أخاه فسلم عليه يخرج من ذنوبه كيوم ولدته أمه، وفي النوازل : إذا أتى بيت غيره لا يدخل حتى يؤذن له، فإن أذن له يدخل ويسلم عليه. ورد السلام واجب واختلفوا في أيهما أفضل البادئ أو الراد الراد أكثر أجراً والأفضل أن يأتي بالواو بأن يقول وعليكم السلام ورحمة الله وبركاته. وفي فتاوى أهواز : السلام سنة على الراكب للراجل في طريق عام أو مفازة فإذا التقيا فأفضلهما الأسبق بالسلام، فإذا التقى الرجل بالمرأة يبدأ الرجل بالسلام، وإن بدأت فيرد عليها السلام إن كانت عجوزاً فبلسانه، وإن كانت شابة فبالإشارة. قال الفقيه أبو الليث : إذا دخل الفقيه على غيره ولم يسلم أثموا. وفي الغياثية : يكره السلام بالسبابة والسنة أن يسلم عليهم بلفظ الجمع، ولو كان المسلم عليه واحداً واختلفوا في السلام على الصبيان قال بعضهم لا يسلم وهو قول الحسن، وقال بعضهم يسلم هو الأفضل وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث، وإذا رد واحد من القوم السلام سقط الباقين. وفي الصيرفية : دخل على زوجته لا يسلم عليها بل هي تسلم عليه فإن لم يكن في البيت أحد فيقول السلام علينا وعلى عباد الله الصالحين ولو على المقابر يقول السلام عليكم أنتم لنا سلف ونحن لكم تبع .اهـ. وفي الخانية : ويكره أن يسلم على من هو في الخلاء ولا يرد عليه السلام. وكذا الأكل والقارئ والمشتغل بالعلم وكذا في الحمام إن كان مكشوف العورة .
And in al-Dhakhira: There is no harm in singing at weddings. And in al-Sirajiyya: Reading poetry, if it does not contain mention of sin or pederasty, is not disliked. And in al-Kafi: If a tenant of a house appears sinful by gathering people for drinking wine, he is prevented, and if he does not desist, he is expelled. And the Imam (Abu Hanifa) did not see any harm in greeting him, in order to distract him from what he is doing. Abu Yusuf disliked greeting him as a form of belittlement. A man summoned by a ruler, who asks him about things, he speaks truth; he may face harm from it; he should not speak except truth unless he fears death or loss of a limb or that his wealth be taken. And if he passes by a group that includes dhimmis or a disbeliever, some say he says 'al-salamu 'ala man ittaba'a al-huda' (peace be upon those who follow guidance), but the correct view is that he says 'al-salamu 'alaykum' and intends the Muslims in his heart. And in al-Tatar Khaniyya: If a Muslim meets his brother and greets him, his sins fall away like the day his mother bore him. And in al-Nawazil: If he comes to another's house, he does not enter until permitted; if permitted, he enters and greets. Responding to the greeting is obligatory. They differed on which is better: the initiator or the responder; the responder has more reward, and it is best to say with 'wa' (and): 'wa 'alaykum al-salam wa rahmatullahi wa barakatuh'. In Fatawa Ahwaz: The greeting is sunnah from the rider to the pedestrian on a public road or desert; when they meet, the better of them is the first to greet. When a man meets a woman, the man initiates the greeting; if she initiates, he responds to her greeting. If she is old, he responds verbally; if young, by gesture. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: If a faqih enters upon someone and does not greet, they sin. In al-Ghiyathiyya: It is disliked to greet with the finger (i.e., by pointing), and the sunnah is to greet them using the plural form, even if the one being greeted is one. They differed about greeting children: some said do not greet (the view of al-Hasan), some said greet, which is better, and this is the view taken by al-Faqih Abu al-Layth. If one of the group returns the greeting, it suffices for the rest. In al-Sayrafiyya: If he enters upon his wife, he does not greet her; rather she greets him. If there is no one in the house, he says 'al-salamu 'alayna wa 'ala 'ibadillah al-salihin' (peace be upon us and upon the righteous servants of Allah). And when at graves, he says 'al-salamu 'alaykum antum lana salaf wa nahnu lakum taba' (peace be upon you, you are our predecessors and we are your followers). In al-Khaniyya: It is disliked to greet someone who is in the toilet, and he should not respond. Similarly for one who is eating, reciting (Quran), engaged in knowledge, and in the bath if the private parts are exposed.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings related to greeting (salam) and associated etiquettes, drawn from multiple Hanafi sources. It covers a wide range of situations: 1) Singing at weddings and reciting poetry are permissible as long as they are free from sin. 2) A sinful tenant can be evicted. 3) Greeting a sinful person: Abu Hanifa allowed it to distract him, while Abu Yusuf disliked it. 4) Speaking truth to a ruler even under threat is required except in extreme danger. 5) When passing a mixed group of Muslims and non-Muslims, the correct greeting is 'al-salamu alaykum' intended for the Muslims. 6) Greeting erases sins. 7) Entering a house requires permission; one should greet after entering. 8) Responding to salam is wajib, and the responder gets more reward; the full phrase with 'wa' is best. 9) The rider should greet the pedestrian; the first to greet is better. 10) A man greets a woman; old woman gets verbal response, young woman gets gesture. 11) A scholar entering without greeting is sinful. 12) Greeting with fingers (i.e., a wave) is disliked; use the plural form even for one. 13) Greeting children: the better view is to greet them. 14) One response from a group suffices. 15) Entering upon wife: she greets; if alone, say 'salam alayna wa ala ibadillah al-salihin'. 16) At graves, a specific greeting. 17) It is disliked to greet someone in the toilet, or while eating, reciting, studying, or in the bath with exposed awrah. These rulings demonstrate the comprehensive nature of Hanafi fiqh in daily interactions.

Key Terms
سلام (salam): The Islamic greeting 'al-salamu alaykum' and its variants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Sunnah muakkadah
B) Wajib
C) Mubah
D) Fard

Key Takeaway
Responding to salam is wajib, and the best response includes 'wa' and the extended blessing.

Greeting Sinners and Non-Muslims: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Rulings on greeting sinners and non-Muslims, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi difference on greeting a sinner and the correct method of greeting a mixed group of Muslims and non-Muslims.

Core Idea
Greeting a sinful person is permissible to reform him, but Abu Yusuf disliked it; greeting non-Muslims requires intention for Muslims.

This lesson explores two specific greeting scenarios: the case of a known sinner (like a wine-drinking tenant) and the case of passing by a group containing both Muslims and dhimmis. The Hanafi school has internal difference on the former, and a clear ruling on the latter.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario of the sinful tenant
Read the case from al-Kafi: a tenant who gathers people for drinking wine is prevented and expelled if he does not desist.
The ruling is about prevention and eviction.
2
Identify the ikhtilaf on greeting him
Abu Hanifa saw no harm in greeting him to distract him. Abu Yusuf disliked greeting as a form of belittlement.
Note the reasoning: one intends distraction, the other intends contempt.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The text does not explicitly prefer one; but generally, Abu Hanifa's view is considered more lenient.
The passage leaves it as an ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the ruling on greeting a mixed group
When passing by a group containing dhimmis or disbelievers, some say to specify 'ala man ittaba'a al-huda', but the correct view is to say 'al-salamu alaykum' and intend the Muslims.
The intention in the heart is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة : لا بأس بالغناء في الأعياد. وفي السراجية : وقراءة الأشعار إذا لم يكن فيه ذكر الفسق والغلام لا يكره. وفي الكافي : مستأجر الدار إذا ظهر منه الفسق بأن يجمع الناس على شرب الخمر يمنع فإذا لم يمتنع يخرج. ولم ير الإمام رحمه الله بالسلام عليه بأساً ليشغله عما هو فيه وكره أبو يوسف السلام تحقيراً له اهـ . رجل يدعوه الأمير فيسأله عن أشياء فيتكلم بما يوافق الحق يناله منه المكروه لا ينبغي له أن يتكلم إلا بالحق إلا أن يخاف القتل أو إتلاف عضو وأن يأخذ ماله. ولو مر على قوم وفيهم أهل الذمة أو كافر قال بعضهم يقول السلام على من ابتع الهدى، والصحيح أنه يقول السلام عليكم وينوي المسلمين في قلبه وفي التتارخانية : إذا استقبل المسلم أخاه فسلم عليه يخرج من ذنوبه كيوم ولدته أمه، وفي النوازل : إذا أتى بيت غيره لا يدخل حتى يؤذن له، فإن أذن له يدخل ويسلم عليه. ورد السلام واجب واختلفوا في أيهما أفضل البادئ أو الراد الراد أكثر أجراً والأفضل أن يأتي بالواو بأن يقول وعليكم السلام ورحمة الله وبركاته. وفي فتاوى أهواز : السلام سنة على الراكب للراجل في طريق عام أو مفازة فإذا التقيا فأفضلهما الأسبق بالسلام، فإذا التقى الرجل بالمرأة يبدأ الرجل بالسلام، وإن بدأت فيرد عليها السلام إن كانت عجوزاً فبلسانه، وإن كانت شابة فبالإشارة. قال الفقيه أبو الليث : إذا دخل الفقيه على غيره ولم يسلم أثموا. وفي الغياثية : يكره السلام بالسبابة والسنة أن يسلم عليهم بلفظ الجمع، ولو كان المسلم عليه واحداً واختلفوا في السلام على الصبيان قال بعضهم لا يسلم وهو قول الحسن، وقال بعضهم يسلم هو الأفضل وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث، وإذا رد واحد من القوم السلام سقط الباقين. وفي الصيرفية : دخل على زوجته لا يسلم عليها بل هي تسلم عليه فإن لم يكن في البيت أحد فيقول السلام علينا وعلى عباد الله الصالحين ولو على المقابر يقول السلام عليكم أنتم لنا سلف ونحن لكم تبع .اهـ. وفي الخانية : ويكره أن يسلم على من هو في الخلاء ولا يرد عليه السلام. وكذا الأكل والقارئ والمشتغل بالعلم وكذا في الحمام إن كان مكشوف العورة .
And in al-Dhakhira: There is no harm in singing at weddings. And in al-Sirajiyya: Reading poetry, if it does not contain mention of sin or pederasty, is not disliked. And in al-Kafi: If a tenant of a house appears sinful by gathering people for drinking wine, he is prevented, and if he does not desist, he is expelled. And the Imam (Abu Hanifa) did not see any harm in greeting him, in order to distract him from what he is doing. Abu Yusuf disliked greeting him as a form of belittlement. A man summoned by a ruler, who asks him about things, he speaks truth; he may face harm from it; he should not speak except truth unless he fears death or loss of a limb or that his wealth be taken. And if he passes by a group that includes dhimmis or a disbeliever, some say he says 'al-salamu 'ala man ittaba'a al-huda' (peace be upon those who follow guidance), but the correct view is that he says 'al-salamu 'alaykum' and intends the Muslims in his heart. And in al-Tatar Khaniyya: If a Muslim meets his brother and greets him, his sins fall away like the day his mother bore him. And in al-Nawazil: If he comes to another's house, he does not enter until permitted; if permitted, he enters and greets. Responding to the greeting is obligatory. They differed on which is better: the initiator or the responder; the responder has more reward, and it is best to say with 'wa' (and): 'wa 'alaykum al-salam wa rahmatullahi wa barakatuh'. In Fatawa Ahwaz: The greeting is sunnah from the rider to the pedestrian on a public road or desert; when they meet, the better of them is the first to greet. When a man meets a woman, the man initiates the greeting; if she initiates, he responds to her greeting. If she is old, he responds verbally; if young, by gesture. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: If a faqih enters upon someone and does not greet, they sin. In al-Ghiyathiyya: It is disliked to greet with the finger (i.e., by pointing), and the sunnah is to greet them using the plural form, even if the one being greeted is one. They differed about greeting children: some said do not greet (the view of al-Hasan), some said greet, which is better, and this is the view taken by al-Faqih Abu al-Layth. If one of the group returns the greeting, it suffices for the rest. In al-Sayrafiyya: If he enters upon his wife, he does not greet her; rather she greets him. If there is no one in the house, he says 'al-salamu 'alayna wa 'ala 'ibadillah al-salihin' (peace be upon us and upon the righteous servants of Allah). And when at graves, he says 'al-salamu 'alaykum antum lana salaf wa nahnu lakum taba' (peace be upon you, you are our predecessors and we are your followers). In al-Khaniyya: It is disliked to greet someone who is in the toilet, and he should not respond. Similarly for one who is eating, reciting (Quran), engaged in knowledge, and in the bath if the private parts are exposed.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings related to greeting (salam) and associated etiquettes, drawn from multiple Hanafi sources. It covers a wide range of situations: 1) Singing at weddings and reciting poetry are permissible as long as they are free from sin. 2) A sinful tenant can be evicted. 3) Greeting a sinful person: Abu Hanifa allowed it to distract him, while Abu Yusuf disliked it. 4) Speaking truth to a ruler even under threat is required except in extreme danger. 5) When passing a mixed group of Muslims and non-Muslims, the correct greeting is 'al-salamu alaykum' intended for the Muslims. 6) Greeting erases sins. 7) Entering a house requires permission; one should greet after entering. 8) Responding to salam is wajib, and the responder gets more reward; the full phrase with 'wa' is best. 9) The rider should greet the pedestrian; the first to greet is better. 10) A man greets a woman; old woman gets verbal response, young woman gets gesture. 11) A scholar entering without greeting is sinful. 12) Greeting with fingers (i.e., a wave) is disliked; use the plural form even for one. 13) Greeting children: the better view is to greet them. 14) One response from a group suffices. 15) Entering upon wife: she greets; if alone, say 'salam alayna wa ala ibadillah al-salihin'. 16) At graves, a specific greeting. 17) It is disliked to greet someone in the toilet, or while eating, reciting, studying, or in the bath with exposed awrah. These rulings demonstrate the comprehensive nature of Hanafi fiqh in daily interactions.

Key Terms
فسق (fisq): Sinfulness; open violation of Islamic law.
أهل الذمة (ahl al-dhimma): Non-Muslim subjects living under Muslim rule with protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did Abu Yusuf think about greeting a sinful tenant?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is recommended
C) It is disliked (makruh)
D) It is haram

Key Takeaway
Greeting a sinner is subject to ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa permitted it for reform, Abu Yusuf disliked it; greeting non-Muslims in a mixed group requires intention for Muslims.

Greeting in Contextual Situations: Homes, Spouse, Graves, and Prohibited States
Contextual greeting rulings for home, spouse, graves, and prohibited states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct greeting etiquette for entering a house, greeting one's spouse, greeting at graves, and situations where greeting is disliked (toilet, eating, reciting, bath).

Core Idea
Greeting etiquette changes with context: permission needed for home, wife greets husband, grave greeting is specific, and greeting is disliked in states of impurity or absorption.

The rulings on greeting extend beyond general encounters. This lesson covers specific contexts: entering someone's home requires permission, spouses have a distinct order, graves have a special formula, and certain states (toilet, eating, studying, bathing) make greeting disliked or prohibitive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for entering a house
Read: one should not enter until permitted; after permission, enter and greet.
Order: request permission → enter → greet.
2
Determine the etiquette between spouses
The wife should greet the husband first; if alone, one says 'salam alayna wa ala ibadillah al-salihin'.
This is from al-Sayrafiyya.
3
Learn the greeting for graves
Say: 'al-salamu alaykum antum lana salaf wa nahnu lakum taba'.
This is the recommended formula.
4
List situations where greeting is disliked
From al-Khaniyya: disliked to greet and respond in the toilet, while eating, reciting Quran, studying, and in the bath with exposed awrah.
The common factor is distraction or lack of proper state.

The General Prohibition of Play and the Chess Dispute
Establishes the prohibition of play and the chess dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi ruling on play, explain the proof from hadith, and evaluate the Shafi'i position on chess.

Core Idea
All play is haram except three; chess is makruh because it resembles Jewish idle play.

This lesson examines the first part of our passage, which establishes a foundational rule: all forms of play are forbidden unless specifically exempted. We then see how this rule applies to chess, creating a clear difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. Understanding this will help you apply the principle of general prohibition and know the limits of permissible recreation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling from the hadith
Read the hadith cited: 'Every play of the son of Adam is haram except three...' What three exceptions are listed? This is the primary proof.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi; it is ahad, so it yields zanni proof.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-argument
Imam al-Shafi'i permitted chess without gambling or neglecting obligations, arguing it sharpens the intellect. State this position clearly.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response relies on the general hadith; chess is included in 'every play' and is specifically associated with Jews. Explain why the school considers it makruh.
The hadith about chess being 'the play of Jews' is not quoted but referenced as famous.
4
Apply the ruling to chess and backgammon
The text explicitly states from al-Muhit that chess, backgammon, and 'fourteen' are disliked because they are the play of Jews. Note the hukm level (makruh tahrimi).
Makruh tahrimi is close to haram; stronger than tanzihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية
لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام كل لعب ابن آدم حرام إلا ثلاثاً ملاعبة الرجل أهله وتأديبه لفرسه ومناضلته لقوسه» (١) وأباح الشافعي الشطرنج من غير قمار ولا إخلال بالواجبات لأنه يذكي الأفهام والحجة عليه ما روينا والأحاديث الواردة في ذلك هي كثيرة شهيرة فتركنا ذكرها لشهرتها. وفي المحيط : ويكره اللعب بالشطرنج والنرد والأربعة عشر لأنها لعب اليهود، ويكره استماع صوت اللهو والضرب به، والوجب على الإنسان أن يجتهد ما أمكن حتى لا يسمع، ولا بأس بضرب الدف في العرس وسئل أبو يوسف عن الدف في غير العرس بأن تضرب المرأة في غير فسق للصبي قال : لا بأس بذلك. وفي الذخيرة : لا بأس بالغناء في الأعياد. وفي السراجية : وقراءة الأشعار إذا لم يكن فيه ذكر الفسق والغلام لا يكره. وفي الكافي : مستأجر الدار إذا ظهر منه الفسق بأن يجمع الناس على شرب الخمر يمنع فإذا لم يمتنع يخرج. ولم ير الإمام رحمه الله بالسلام عليه بأساً ليشغله عما هو فيه وكره أبو يوسف السلام تحقيراً له اهـ .
Book of Disliked Acts. Because of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Every play of the son of Adam is haram except three: a man playing with his wife, training his horse, and his archery with his bow.' And al-Shafi'i permitted chess without gambling or neglect of obligations because it sharpens the intellect. The proof against him is what we narrated, and the hadiths on that are many and famous, so we omitted mentioning them due to their fame. In al-Muhit: Playing chess, backgammon, and 'fourteen' is disliked because it is the play of Jews. Listening to the sound of idle play and striking it is disliked. It is incumbent on a person to strive as much as possible not to hear it. There is no harm in beating the duff (frame drum) during a wedding. Abu Yusuf was asked about the duff other than at a wedding, such as a woman beating it without sin for a child; he said: There is no harm in that. In al-Dhakhira: There is no harm in singing during Eid. In al-Sirajiyya: Reciting poetry if it does not contain mention of sin and boys is not disliked. In al-Kafi: If a tenant of a house shows sinfulness by gathering people for drinking wine, he is prevented; if he does not desist, he is expelled. The Imam (Abu Hanifa), may Allah have mercy on him, saw no harm in greeting him to distract him from what he is in, but Abu Yusuf disliked greeting him as a way of degrading him (the sinner). End.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Disliked Acts (Kitab al-Karahiyya). It begins with a hadith that establishes a general principle: all forms of play are haram except three specific activities: playing with one's spouse, training one's horse, and archery. This is a primary proof. The text then presents a disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who allowed chess without gambling or neglect of duties. The Hanafi response is that the general prohibition applies. The author then cites various rulings: chess, backgammon, and a game called 'fourteen' are disliked (makruh) because it is the play of Jews. Listening to idle play and musical instruments is also disliked, and one should strive not to hear it. However, the duff (frame drum) is permissible at weddings, and even outside weddings if done by a woman without sin for a child, according to Abu Yusuf. Singing during Eid is permissible, and reciting poetry without immoral content is not disliked. Additionally, if a tenant engages in sinful activities like gathering people for wine, he should be prevented; if he persists, he is evicted. There is a difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding greeting such a sinner: Abu Hanifa allowed it to distract him, while Abu Yusuf disliked it as degradation. This shows the nuanced approach of the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
اللعب (al-la'b): Play or game; specifically prohibited except for three exceptions.
الشطرنج (al-shatranj): Chess; a game of strategy.
النرد (al-nard): Backgammon; a dice game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on playing chess without gambling or neglect of obligations?
A) Haram
B) Makruh
C) Mubah
D) Sunnah

Key Takeaway
All play is prohibited except three specific activities; chess is makruh tahrimi due to its connection with Jewish idle play and heedlessness.

Permissibility of Duff, Singing, and Poetry in Specific Contexts
Rulings on permissibility of duff, singing, and poetry under specific conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on duff, singing, and poetry, and identify the conditions that make them permissible or disliked.

Core Idea
Duff is permissible at weddings and for children without sin; singing at Eid and poetry without immoral content are also allowed.

The previous lesson established a general prohibition of play, but this lesson shows that certain forms of entertainment are permitted under specific conditions. The duff (frame drum) is a common instrument; the school distinguishes between its use in weddings and other contexts. Similarly, singing and poetry are not inherently disliked unless they involve sin. Understanding these nuances helps avoid unnecessary restrictions while remaining within Islamic boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on duff in weddings
The text says: 'There is no harm in beating the duff during a wedding.' This establishes permissibility in a marriage context.
The duff is a simple frame drum; stringed instruments are not included.
2
Examine the exceptional case from Abu Yusuf
When asked about duff outside a wedding for a child without sin, Abu Yusuf also permitted it. Note the conditions: done by a woman, not leading to sin, and for a child.
This shows an extension of permissibility when the intent is innocent.
3
Determine the ruling on singing and poetry
Singing during Eid is permissible. Reciting poetry is not disliked as long as it contains no mention of sin or young boys (ghilman).
The mention of 'ghilman' indicates avoiding any suggestion of immorality.
4
Apply the conditions to other scenarios
The common condition is that the activity must not involve sin (fisq) or neglect of obligations. Any entertainment that leads to immorality or heedlessness would be prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية
لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام كل لعب ابن آدم حرام إلا ثلاثاً ملاعبة الرجل أهله وتأديبه لفرسه ومناضلته لقوسه» (١) وأباح الشافعي الشطرنج من غير قمار ولا إخلال بالواجبات لأنه يذكي الأفهام والحجة عليه ما روينا والأحاديث الواردة في ذلك هي كثيرة شهيرة فتركنا ذكرها لشهرتها. وفي المحيط : ويكره اللعب بالشطرنج والنرد والأربعة عشر لأنها لعب اليهود، ويكره استماع صوت اللهو والضرب به، والوجب على الإنسان أن يجتهد ما أمكن حتى لا يسمع، ولا بأس بضرب الدف في العرس وسئل أبو يوسف عن الدف في غير العرس بأن تضرب المرأة في غير فسق للصبي قال : لا بأس بذلك. وفي الذخيرة : لا بأس بالغناء في الأعياد. وفي السراجية : وقراءة الأشعار إذا لم يكن فيه ذكر الفسق والغلام لا يكره. وفي الكافي : مستأجر الدار إذا ظهر منه الفسق بأن يجمع الناس على شرب الخمر يمنع فإذا لم يمتنع يخرج. ولم ير الإمام رحمه الله بالسلام عليه بأساً ليشغله عما هو فيه وكره أبو يوسف السلام تحقيراً له اهـ .
Book of Disliked Acts. Because of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Every play of the son of Adam is haram except three: a man playing with his wife, training his horse, and his archery with his bow.' And al-Shafi'i permitted chess without gambling or neglect of obligations because it sharpens the intellect. The proof against him is what we narrated, and the hadiths on that are many and famous, so we omitted mentioning them due to their fame. In al-Muhit: Playing chess, backgammon, and 'fourteen' is disliked because it is the play of Jews. Listening to the sound of idle play and striking it is disliked. It is incumbent on a person to strive as much as possible not to hear it. There is no harm in beating the duff (frame drum) during a wedding. Abu Yusuf was asked about the duff other than at a wedding, such as a woman beating it without sin for a child; he said: There is no harm in that. In al-Dhakhira: There is no harm in singing during Eid. In al-Sirajiyya: Reciting poetry if it does not contain mention of sin and boys is not disliked. In al-Kafi: If a tenant of a house shows sinfulness by gathering people for drinking wine, he is prevented; if he does not desist, he is expelled. The Imam (Abu Hanifa), may Allah have mercy on him, saw no harm in greeting him to distract him from what he is in, but Abu Yusuf disliked greeting him as a way of degrading him (the sinner). End.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Disliked Acts (Kitab al-Karahiyya). It begins with a hadith that establishes a general principle: all forms of play are haram except three specific activities: playing with one's spouse, training one's horse, and archery. This is a primary proof. The text then presents a disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who allowed chess without gambling or neglect of duties. The Hanafi response is that the general prohibition applies. The author then cites various rulings: chess, backgammon, and a game called 'fourteen' are disliked (makruh) because it is the play of Jews. Listening to idle play and musical instruments is also disliked, and one should strive not to hear it. However, the duff (frame drum) is permissible at weddings, and even outside weddings if done by a woman without sin for a child, according to Abu Yusuf. Singing during Eid is permissible, and reciting poetry without immoral content is not disliked. Additionally, if a tenant engages in sinful activities like gathering people for wine, he should be prevented; if he persists, he is evicted. There is a difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding greeting such a sinner: Abu Hanifa allowed it to distract him, while Abu Yusuf disliked it as degradation. This shows the nuanced approach of the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
الدف (al-duff): Frame drum; permissible in weddings and for children without sin.
الغناء (al-ghina'): Singing; specifically singing during Eid is permissible.
الشعر (al-shi'r): Poetry; reciting is not disliked if free of sin and mention of boys.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Whenever a woman does it
B) For a child, without sin
C) Only during Eid
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Duff, singing at Eid, and poetry are permissible as long as they are free from sin and appropriate in context.

Dealing with a Sinful Tenant and Greeting a Sinner
Rulings on evicting a sinful tenant and the debate on greeting a sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on evicting a sinful tenant and state the difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding greeting a sinner.

Core Idea
A tenant who gathers people for wine must be expelled; greeting a sinner is allowed to distract him, but Abu Yusuf disliked it as degradation.

The final section of our passage deals with social interactions with sinners. When a tenant openly engages in sin (wine drinking), the landlord must intervene. There is also a subtle difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on greeting such a person: one sees it as a means of distraction, the other as a form of respect that should be withheld. This lesson teaches both the legal obligation to stop sin and the adab (etiquette) of dealing with sinners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation regarding a sinful tenant
If a tenant gathers people for drinking wine (a major sin), the landlord must prevent him. If he does not desist, he is to be expelled. This is from al-Kafi.
This ruling shows using property to enable sin is impermissible.
2
Analyze the different views on greeting the sinner
Abu Hanifa saw no harm in greeting such a sinner to distract him from his sin. Abu Yusuf disliked greeting him, viewing it as degrading the sinner by acknowledging him while he is sinning.
The disagreement is over whether greeting helps or harms the situation.
3
Determine the practical application
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is not given explicitly, but the text presents both views. In practice, one may choose based on circumstances: if greeting might stop the sin, it could be preferred; otherwise, withholding greeting may be better.

Source Text
